- **Phosphorescent ball near vehicle**  
A bright green, phosphorescent ball dropped from the sky behind a minibus, rolling toward a teacher's car. When it made contact, it gave the vehicle a strong shock and illuminated everything around it in bright green, but no physical damage or indications of lightning was found later.

- **Lightning striking USAF jet**  
Two witnesses observed lightning striking a low-flying USAF jet, causing vapors to form around the back of the plane. Shortly after, a dazzling blue-white ball entered a nearby factory, creating sparks and illuminating the interior for about two seconds before it exited with a loud bang.

- **Ball of white fire near tree**  
A woman observed a 'ball of white fire' moving horizontally along her drive during heavy rainfall without lightning. Upon striking an oak tree, it exploded and emitted waves of lightning towards the ground, scattering wood splinters over a wide area.

- **Luminous vertical column during storm**  
A witness described seeing a luminous vertical column with a peculiar bluish glow during a violent electrical storm, lasting a few seconds. Later, a spherical object of similar luminescence moved rapidly, showing undulations in its course, but made no noise.

- **Silver-colored ball with ribbon of light**  
A silver-colored ball was seen shooting across the sky, accompanied by a 'swoosh' sound, before landing with a deafening explosion. Interestingly, there was no mark left on the ground where it impacted, and its rapid movement left an impression on the witnesses.

- **Floating ball of lightning with rings**  
A group of campers observed a ball of lightning that seemed to float, increasing in size but not reaching them. When it vanished, it left behind two concentric rings, with a strange red illumination appearing in the sky afterward.

- **Translucent ball jumping indoors**  
During a thunderstorm, a translucent ball of light appeared indoors, jumping and then rolling across the floor before exploding near a stove. It left a deep groove in its path and an ozone smell but no marks on the floor.

- **Ball blasting through factory wall**  
A large ball of lightning entered a factory through a steel-reinforced window, shattering it without fusion and blasting a large hole through a wall before exiting. The immense roar accompanied its passage, leaving behind debris scattered outside the factory.

- **Bright flash of light with smoke**  
A sudden and bright circular flash of light was observed during thunderstorms, which silently 'popped out' leaving behind smoke. Interestingly, this phenomenon occurred without any lightning associated with it, highlighting its mysterious nature.

- **Orange-reddish ball during thunderstorm**  
A woman described seeing an orange-reddish ball resembling a bowling ball that exploded noisily as it approached her vehicle. Notably, it left no evidence of impact on the ground after the explosion, contributing to its odd nature.

- **Yellow-orange ball hovering**  
A scientist reported a bright yellow and red ball hovering above the floor in a room during a storm before disappearing with a loud pop. This event created a distinct odor of ozone and appeared to have captured the attention of a nearby cat.

- **Ball lightning explosions over ocean**  
Officers observed reddish-white balls of lightning appearing above clouds, described as intense explosions that flashed in chains across the sky and sea. Unlike the typical ground-level occurrences of ball lightning, this event displayed a remarkable and fierce aerial behavior.

- **Detached luminous sphere observed in lightning discharges**  
A research team recorded video of a lightning strike that resulted in a ball-shaped image appearing detached from the main channel. Initial estimations suggested this luminous sphere to be about five meters in diameter, representing an unusual sight during the storm.

- **Large ball of lightning discharging**  
A large ball of lightning was reported to have discharged towards a drilling platform during a storm. This occurrence was part of an impending squall, showcasing the unpredictable nature of ball lightning in extreme weather conditions.
# Complex Events [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- A possible lightning strike on the drilling platform was reported when a luminous ball seemingly passed through the legs of the platform. Upon inspection, it was found that no damage had occurred.

- The luminous phenomenon was circular, emerging from a low cloud base and comparable in size to a dust bin lid. Its velocity was remarkable, estimated at about 100 miles per hour, which is unusual for typical ball lightning.

# Anomalous Sphere Observation [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- R. Cahill captured an image of a luminous sphere that appeared to be ball lightning during a lightning storm. The sphere entered the camera's field of view shortly after a lightning flash and traversed the screen diagonally in about 1.5 seconds.

- The characteristics of the observed sphere were summarized as a dull-red, toroidal object. Experiments in slow motion revealed its coherent structure, exhibiting eight-fold symmetry and a small, non-luminous inner radius.

# Critiques on Ball Lightning Claims [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- S. Campbell, a critic of ball lightning claims, examined the video capturing the luminous sphere and posited that it was merely the camera’s stop-plane illuminated by distant car lights. He successfully replicated the supposed ball lightning image, suggesting that it lacked credibility.

- Campbell challenges the validity of the claims made by Jennison et al., stating that ball lightning reports are primarily anecdotal. He posits that there is insufficient instrumental evidence to confirm the phenomenon's reality.

# General Observations on Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- A general treatment of ball lightning by M.A. Uman describes it as "wild and wonderful," indicating a need for further theoretical constraints correlating with its apparent properties. Uman’s overview emphasizes the unresolved nature of ball lightning in scientific discourse.

- D.J. Turner extensively reviews the characteristics and literature of ball lightning, proposing his own theory. This ongoing exploration highlights the diverse interpretations and understanding of ball lightning phenomena in recent studies.

# Surveys of Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- M. Stenhoff's survey of about 200 ball lightning reports concluded that approximately 80% of sightings could be explained by other means. His work raises doubts about the credibility of ball lightning and calls into question the reliability of earlier statistical studies.

- In contrast, V.L. Bychkov and colleagues report on over 2,000 ball lightning accounts, though their findings lack scrutiny regarding the observations' validity. This disparity among researchers underscores the complexities and differing opinions surrounding ball lightning's existence.

# Theoretical Approaches to Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- Various hypotheses about ball lightning have emerged over the years, including linked magnetic loops and electromagnetic knots. These theories point to the diverse explanations sought by scientists and laymen trying to understand this anomalous phenomenon.

- D.J. Turner proposes that ball lightning possesses an onion-like structure, suggesting complexity in its formation. This adds to the growing list of speculative theories attempting to elucidate the nature of ball lightning.
# Chapter 2:80, July 1952 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly: Ball Lightning Observed by Nebraska Family**  
  This phenomenon was recorded by a family in Nebraska in July 1952. The unusual sighting involved a glowing sphere that defied conventional explanation, captivating those who witnessed it.

- **Anomaly: Curious Effects of Globular Lightning**  
  Documented in Nature in 1938, this anomaly details observations of various effects associated with globular lightning. It highlights the enigmatic behavior of this phenomenon and its curious interactions with the environment.

- **Anomaly: Ball Lightning in a Cloud?**  
  This 1942 report raises the question of whether ball lightning can manifest within cloud formations. The study presents anomalous observations and discussions surrounding the occurrence of lightning balls in atmospheric conditions.

- **Anomaly: Globe Lightning**  
  From a 1945 publication in Nature, this entry refers to instances where lightning is perceived as glowing globes. Researchers explored the properties and behaviors of these elusive globes of lightning, attempting to understand their nature.

- **Anomaly: The Enigma of Ball Lightning**  
  Published in New Scientist in 1972, this article delves into the perplexing characteristics and occurrences of ball lightning. The findings juxtapose anecdotal evidence with scientific inquiry into this captivating phenomenon.

- **Anomaly: Optical and Acoustical Detection of Ball Lightning**  
  Discussed in a 1971 Nature article, this research focuses on the methods used to detect ball lightning using optical and acoustical techniques. The findings emphasize both the visibility and audibility of ball lightning, furthering our understanding of this anomaly.

- **Anomaly: The Widecombe Calamity**  
  This report details a meteorological event in 1966 that may have included ball lightning phenomena. It discusses the unusual and catastrophic occurrences during a severe weather event, hinting at the complexities of reporting ball lightning.

- **Anomaly: Tuminous Phenomena in Nocturnal Tornadoes**  
  This 1966 article in Science explores bright phenomena appearing during tornadoes at night. The striking luminous effects suggest a relationship with tumultuous weather conditions, provoking further curiosity around the phenomenon.

- **Anomaly: Fireball Cuts Off 2500 TV Sets**  
  A detailed report regarding an unusual fireball sighted in 1970 that interrupted electrical systems, affecting a large number of televisions. This incident highlights the destructive potential and unpredictable nature of electrical manifestations such as ball lightning.

- **Anomaly: A Report on Ball Lightning**  
  This 1972 research document presents a comprehensive investigation into ball lightning. It synthesizes various observations and theories, contributing to the broader understanding of electrical phenomena.

# Chapter GLB1 Ordinary Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly: Not Ball Lightning**  
  A 1899 review in Monthly Weather Review dismisses certain phenomena as non-ball lightning. This classification and inquiry into what constitutes ball lightning serve to clarify the distinctions within atmospheric electrical events.

- **Anomaly: Ball of Electric Fire**  
  Recorded in Science in 1885, this early depiction of ball lightning describes it as an intense sphere of fiery energy. The characterization underscores the historic fascination and scientific effort to explain this electrifying spectacle.

- **Anomaly: Fireballs**  
  A report from 1909 details experiences with fireballs, adding to the existing body of literature on similar phenomena. The recorded accounts suggest wide-ranging manifestations that may relate to ball lightning.

# Chapter GLB2 Spiked Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly: Ball Lightning with Projections or Spikes**  
  A unique description of ball lightning adorned with spikes or protuberances highlights its complex nature. The spike formation suggests potential internal forces at play, raising further questions about stability and detonation risks.

- **Anomaly: Luminous Spheres with Diverging Rays**  
  This phenomenon describes luminous spheres that exhibit long, radial rays, particularly noticeable during explosive events. The rays' presence may indicate the internal structure of ball lightning or signify a prelude to disintegration.

- **Anomaly: Cleavage of Ball Lightning**  
  Reports suggest instances where ball lightning exhibited a distinct cleavage pattern during disintegration. This phenomenon presents an intriguing aspect of ball lightning's behavior, inviting analysis into its physical properties.

- **Anomaly: Ball Lightning with Fragmentation**  
  The occurrence of fragmenting ball lightning introduces an additional layer of complexity. This illustrates that ball lightning can undergo disintegration, aligning with observations of instability in its structure.
# Rayed Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **X4.** On Mt. Bohul, Russia, a geodesic party reported seeing an extremely bright violet ball surrounded by rays about two yards long, which struck the peak. This phenomenon repeated with a second and third ball, flooding the summit in an electric light for no less than four hours.

- **X5.** On August 23, 1878, in London, England, ball lightning manifested as bursts with a splendid mass of whitish-blue rays. This reported spectacle caught the attention of witnesses during a particularly stormy period.

- **X6.** On April 15, 1916, at the Puy de Dome Observatory in France, several fireballs exploded, emitting tongues of fire in all directions. The vivid explosions likely created a terrifying and awe-inspiring scene for any onlookers.

- **X7.** In Nioheim, Prussia, an undefined date mentions a ball lightning event where a sphere the size of a cannonball erupted amidst rays. This enigmatic appearance added to the intrigue surrounding ball lightning phenomena.

- **X8.** In June 1849, during a significant cholera outbreak in Paris, a large red globe resembling the moon was observed descending toward a tree. Witnesses noted its slow descent and witnessed a horrific explosion that caused considerable damage.

- **X9.** On October 20, 1962, a reddish fire-like ball was seen moving across the street in Madras, India, just after violent thunderbolts. This fireball, which appeared above the ground, astonished residents as it spun and caused damage to an adjoining house.

- **X10.** On May 31, 1982, in Wakefield, England, a simultaneous appearance of two ball lightning manifestations of different types occurred during a heavy thunderstorm. One appeared as electric blue flashes, while the other was described as a hovering orange fireball.

- **X11.** In 1980, an unusual occurrence at Shotton steelworks in England involved a large ball of lightning bouncing around a yard, creating an impressive spectacle. The description highlighted the strange physical effects left behind, including a clear pattern resembling a dartboard on the shed where it had struck.

- **X12.** On January 8, 1992, at Minneapolis-St. Paul Airport, a pilot reported encountering a small incandescent sphere surrounded by a bluish-white plasma cloud. The sharp bang upon contact with the aircraft prompted an immediate investigation of the phenomenon.
# Miniature Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **X1. August 17, 1921. Eastbourne, England.** Two ladies witnessed a ball of light flash past a knife on the table, traveling about 9 inches away and 3 inches high. The ball extinguished with a spitting sound upon touching the tablecloth, leaving no trace.

- **X2. June 19, 1924. Baltimore, Maryland.** Mrs. Ames saw a small fireball about the size of a pecan rise toward her finger during a thunderstorm. The fireball disconnected just as a flash of lightning occurred outside, vanishing instantly.

- **X3. August 1885. Sotteville, France.** Several small balls about the size of peas fell to the ground during a storm, igniting upon contact and emitting violet flames. Witnesses reported more than twenty such fireballs during this event.

# Giant Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **X1. November 1, 1864. Florence, Italy.** Witnesses saw a white globe of fire larger than the full moon hanging motionless in the air, shifting colors before suddenly disappearing. The immense light illuminated the area around it, creating the illusion of moonlight.

- **X2. February 8, 1906. Beccles, England.** A huge circle of light appeared during a thunderstorm, perceived as multiple times the diameter of a setting sun, and was accompanied by a loud explosion. Nearby witnesses described the event with slight discrepancies regarding color.

- **X3. July 9, 1975. Albany, New York.** Observers reported a large fireball appearing near a factory during multiple lightning strikes, remaining bright and fuzzy in appearance. The ball's brightness matched that of the lightning, and it vanished simultaneously with the last stroke.

- **X4. June 8, 1977. Dyfed, West Wales, U.K.** A very large fireball about the size of a bus descended from a cloud, emitting intense light before flickering out. The phenomenon was accompanied by severe static on the radio and seemed to disturb nearby animals.

- **X5. November 4, 1749. North Atlantic Ocean.** A large fireball rolled over the water and exploded upon hitting a ship, causing significant damage. This was an unusual event that marked its destructive nature.

- **X6. June 26, 1921. St. John's Wood, England.** An incandescent mass floated in the air for an extended period beneath the clouds, remaining stationary and drawing observers' curiosity. Its appearance raised questions about its nature and origin.

- **X7. August 30, 1930. Nebraska.** A lavender-colored shapeless mass floated downward near a squall line, while globular structures traveled along power lines. The phenomenon presented unusual characteristics amidst the brewing storm.

- **X8. July 22, 1951. Dorset, England.** A large, non-spherical mass of blinding light hovered above the ground before fading out, measuring about 1 yard in all dimensions. Its sudden disappearance raised questions about its composition and nature.

- **X9. November 11, 1940. London, Ontario, Canada.** A couple mistaken a fiery-red mass as a burning car, only to discover it was a large fireball moving along the road. Its sudden lift-off and explosion left behind a white precipitate that intrigued the witnesses.

- **X10. July 6, 1983. Buckinghamshire, England.** Several travelers saw a giant blue ball of light while on the M1 Motorway, entering detailed descriptions of its characteristics. The observation was documented alongside a sketch indicating its size and clarity.

- **X11. November 24, 1987. Tulsa, Oklahoma.** Witnesses reported seeing a bluish sphere near an oil refinery post-lightning strike, which fragmented in a loud detonation within seconds. The weather was stormy, contributing to the dramatic nature of the event.

- **X12. June 28, 1989, near Silbury Hill, Wiltshire, England.** A large orange ball of light was observed descending into a wheatfield, leading to the formation of an unusual crop circle. The incident's timing and location sparked discussions about natural versus paranormal explanations.

- **X13. June 8, 1977. Fishguard, Dyfed, Wales, United Kingdom.** A brilliantly colored, large ball of light was seen floating down a hillside from a cumulus cloud, accompanied by intense light and disturbances in nearby wildlife. Observers noted its low altitude and distinctive shape.

- **X14. July 26, 1995. Ruislip, Middlesex, England.** An enormous fireball spotted across the road from a couple in their car displayed an orange-yellow hue before exploding violently. This incident caused damage to electrical equipment nearby, underscoring its impact.

# Transparent Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Description:** Transparent masses that resemble ordinary ball lightning but exhibit low energy density, allowing them to be seen through. This phenomenon may share characteristics with nocturnal lights, blurring the line between different types of luminous phenomena.

- **Background:** The origins and nature of transparent ball lightning remain obscure, as it can be confused with will o' the wisps and other ghost lights. Comparisons between the two illuminate the complexity of classifying such phenomena and their related characteristics.
# Examples of Transparent Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- **X1. September 1910. Mazoc, South Rhodesia.** A luminous and transparent ball of fire, resembling an electric light, was observed hovering over a road for several minutes. It cast no light beyond itself and emitted no noise or detectable smell, disappearing without a trace afterward.

- **X2. June 1, 1984. Nottingham, England.** A glass-like ball of fire was seen floating in a bowl of water in a closed kitchen. It produced a 'boiling noise' followed by a crash sound, and after the witness fled, an explosion occurred, leaving no damage behind.

# Examples of Fragmenting Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- **X1. November 16, 1857. Charleston, South Carolina.** A red, fiery ball was observed slowly descending and striking a wooden fence, where it split into three parts before disappearing. This occurrence happened in a very controlled manner, giving witnesses time to observe the strange event unfold.

- **X2. July 17, 1890. Surbiton, England.** A large ball of reddish-yellow fire was suspended in mid-air, which suddenly changed color and exploded, releasing dozens of smaller fiery splashes. The intense brightness experienced left the observer dazed for several moments afterward.

- **X3. April 13, 1904. Essex, England.** Eyewitnesses reported seeing a ball of fire that emitted a blinding flash followed by a crashing explosion, which produced several distinct, circular holes in the ground. These holes were clean-cut and perfectly round, resembling those made by a boring tool.

- **X4. October 8, 1919. Salina, Kansas.** A brilliant display of ball lightning exploded at a busy street intersection, damaging a building by tearing out bricks and shattering windows. The explosion shot flaming spheres in all directions, which behaved erratically along electric wires.

- **X5. 1960s. Neustadt, Germany.** During a thunderstorm, a spherical plasma ball appeared above a tree, disintegrating into smaller spheres upon contact. This event was repeated shortly after in the same location, highlighting its strange consistency during the storm.

- **X6. July 28, 1973. Rabaul, Papua-New Guinea.** A ball of lightning traversed the sky, leaving a meteoric tail behind before it exploded into multiple 'fingers' of light. These fingers then retracted back into the original ball, retracing their paths into the cloud.

- **X7. July 16, 1750. Surrey, England.** A large fireball was reported to divide into many parts, demonstrating a clear example of fragmentation. Witnesses noted the unusual visual spectacle of the ball's division.

- **X8. August 1881. No place given.** A bright elongated fireball was seen emerging from a cloud, with a portion breaking off and falling to the ground. This spectacle left observers marveling at its sudden appearance and disappearance.

- **X9. August 1881. Connersville, Indiana.** A lightning bolt was reported to fragment into several balls that rolled around the ground, indicating an unusual form of lightning behavior. This rare sight raised questions about the nature and mechanics of lightning.

- **X10. February 24, 1884. Amiens, France.** A brilliant mass of light was observed dividing into numerous harmless glowing globules. This event contributed to the fascination surrounding the properties of ball lightning.

- **X11. June 10, 1891. Woodstock, England.** Witnesses described a fireball bursting into glowing fragments, which indicated an extraordinary display of energy dissipation. The fragmentation added to the mysterious nature of the phenomenon.

- **X12. August 9, 1893. Kingstown, England.** A fireball was observed darting across the water's surface, leaving smaller balls behind. This behavior added to existing accounts of unpredictable ball lightning activities.

- **X13. Circa 1895. Culday, Ireland.** A large shining mass approached a boy and then burst into several fragments, causing injury to his hand. This incident highlighted the physical impacts that such phenomena could have on individuals.

- **X14. Circa 1907. Minnesota.** A fireball struck a tree and scattered in all directions, demonstrating a dramatic display of fragmentation.Witnesses were left in awe of the sheer force and scattering of the fireball.

- **X15. Circa 1920. Marietta, Ohio.** A ball of lightning exploded, launching brilliant particles in every direction. This eruption contributed to the ongoing intrigue surrounding explosive ball lightning events.

- **X16. September 10, 1924. Finley, North Dakota.** A dull, greenish-yellow ball of light appeared and subsequently split into smaller parts, illustrating the unpredictable behavior of such phenomena. This division further complicates the existing understanding of ball lightning.

- **X17. June 10, 1948. London, England.** A reddish-white fireball, twice the size of a football, exploded, sending fragments flying in various directions. The significant explosion cemented the fireball's dramatic presentation.

- **X18. No date given. Lanarkshire, England.** A ball of fire settled on a thatched roof and gradually disintegrated into pieces. Witnesses observed the slow breakdown of the phenomenon.

- **X19. November 1825. Newton, Ohio.** Two ladies walking in the road were suddenly enveloped in a mass of light, which descended from the sky and exploded into smaller balls. This peculiar event left no trace of heat or contact.

- **X20. April 21, 1964. Stockton-on-Tees, England.** A shimmering sphere was seen falling from a cloud, changing direction before causing an explosion upon landing. The aftermath resulted in significant damage to a house's roof.

- **X21. August 24, 1895. No place given.** A whitish globe observed during a storm touched the ground and split into two smaller globes before disappearing. The phenomenon resulted in peculiar occurrences affecting nearby sheep, which were found alive but distressed.
# Ball Lightning Materialization GLB10 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly X3**: On August 7, 1951, an aircraft over Southern England experienced a loud bang and an intense flash of light. Observers reported seeing a white floating ball of fire in different locations within the cockpit, with simultaneous disappearance and explosion.

- **Anomaly X4**: On July 27, 1952, in Hamburg, Germany, a fist-sized gleaming sphere appeared after a lightning strike and penetrated a closed window without damage. The sphere made a sudden turn, floated further into the room, and burst with a deafening explosion, leaving no damage and a typical odor of electrical discharge.

- **Anomaly X5**: In 1960, an Air Force tanker encountered a ball of yellow-white light that emerged through the windshield and moved quickly through the cabin. Despite expectations of an explosion, no sound accompanied its passage, and crew members noted an excited description from the Boom operator about the ball rolling off into the night after moving through the cargo compartment.

- **Anomaly X6**: On March 19, 1963, an aircraft over the U.S. East Coast observed a glowing sphere that emerged from the pilot's cabin and passed down the aisle. The object maintained a symmetrical shape, did not radiate heat, and displayed a blue-white color, with its physical properties carefully assessed relative to its surroundings.

- **Anomaly X7**: On November 6, 1963, in Nottingham, England, a father witnessed a small egg-shaped ball of brilliant light that spread into a curtain of dark green light across the room. The phenomenon culminated with a loud bang as the light vanished, resembling the sound of a rifle shot.

- **Anomaly X8**: On August 25, 1965, in Dunnellon, Florida, a basketball-sized ball of lightning appeared during an overcast sky. It moved quickly after a woman swatted a fly, accompanied by a report similar to a shotgun blast, and the entire incident lasted only seconds.

- **Anomaly X9**: In Warley, England, on August 8, 1975, a sphere of light approximately 10 cm across appeared over a kitchen cooker during a thunderstorm. The glowing ball experienced burning heat and created a singeing smell, ultimately causing damage to the witness's hand and clothing.

- **Anomaly X10**: In 1753, St. Petersburg, Russia, G. W. Richman encountered a fist-sized pale blue ball of lightning that emerged from his apparatus and resulted in his death. This incident marked an early and tragic account of ball lightning's impact on individuals.

- **Anomaly X11**: On July 16, 1905, a large fireball appeared inside a closed house in Paducah, Kentucky, causing a notable event but lacking detailed description. This instance highlights the occurrences of unexplained fireballs in living spaces.

- **Anomaly X12**: On June 19, 1924, in Baltimore, Maryland, miniature ball lightning materialized in a closed room. This observation adds to the understanding of how ball lightning can occur in confined spaces.

- **Anomaly X13**: In December 1929, an orange fireball exploded in a closed house in Liverpool, England, leaving behind a distinct odor of ozone. The explosion exemplifies the potential hazards associated with ball lightning phenomena.

- **Anomaly X14**: On June 9, 1930, in Auckland, New Zealand, there was an explosion accompanied by a bright yellow flash in a closed kitchen attributed to ball lightning. This incident reinforces the connection between potential explosive behavior and ball lightning activity.

- **Anomaly X15**: In 1956 in Katsina, Nigeria, a tennis ball-sized fireball appeared within a house built of thick mud walls. The phenomenon suggests that even densely constructed environments are not immune to the effects of ball lightning.

- **Anomaly X16**: At an unspecified date and location, ball lightning suddenly appeared on the floor of an aircraft's passenger cabin. It ran down the aisle and disappeared with a bang, indicating a potential aviation safety concern.

- **Anomaly X17**: In 1914, during a severe thunderstorm, a fireball emerged from electrical machinery, measuring 15-18 inches in diameter. The event was marked by splashing behavior, indicating an unusual physical interaction with the environment.

- **Anomaly X18**: On April 20, 1915, in Columbia, Missouri, Mr. Seaton observed a small, palish red ball of light crossing a space between a telephone and window during a thunderstorm. The ball's floating motion and disappearance without explosion or damage presents a curious case of ball lightning behavior.

- **Anomaly X19**: On July 24, 1994, Mr. and Mrs. Langer witnessed a large white ball, initially appearing in a sitting room, that bounced and exploded with a loud noise. The phenomenon's rapid brightness change and resulting minor marks on the carpet indicate a non-destructive incident.

- **Anomaly X20**: On July 3, 1982, during a thunderstorm in Berkshire, England, Mr. Bell saw a glowing sphere materialize before him and move around the room. Its subsequent exit through a bathroom window caused a loud bang and the explosion of a nearby television, demonstrating ball lightning's potential for destructive encounters.
# Electromagnetic Effects  [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly 1**: Ordinary lightning often causes electromagnetic effects, whereas electromagnetic effects from ball lightning are rarely documented. The scarcity of reports on ball lightning’s expected electromagnetic effects may make it an anomaly.

- **Anomaly 2**: The energy of ball lightning might be temporarily stored within an electromagnetic bottle, which also confines its electromagnetic fields. This theoretical idea remains unexplained as it does not align with conventional understandings of physical phenomena.

# Ball Lightning with Apparent Internal Structure  [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly 1**: Reports of ball lightning exhibiting internal structures, such as snake-like fibers of light, are rare yet consistent. The lack of any established theory to explain these bizarre snake-like features further marks their anomalous nature.

- **Anomaly 2**: The luminous fibers observed in some ball lightning phenomena could be transitory plasma currents or wave-like structures on the surface of a plasma sphere. This remains a speculation as no established theories currently account for such structures in ball lightning. 

# Unusual Physiological Effects of Ball Lightning  [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly 1**: Encountering ball lightning has been reported to cause symptoms such as headache, nausea, neck pain, memory loss, and disorientation. These symptoms, although typical of electrical shock, raise questions regarding potential nuclear origins of ball lightning due to their similarity to radiation sickness.

- **Anomaly 2**: The peculiar occurrence where multiple individuals experience extensive swellings and skin reactions without any visible damage to their surroundings suggests a phenomenon not completely understood. The symptoms reported in certain historical encounters closely resemble those associated with overexposure to nuclear radiation, despite occurring before the establishment of known radiation science.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly 1**: Seven wounds were discovered on the narrator's body, worse than burns, with pieces of muscle torn out to the bone. Similar injuries were reported for Shigin, Kaprov, and Bashkirov, indicating a potential violent event or unnatural cause.

- **Anomaly 2**: Oleg Korovin was killed by ball lightning, possibly due to his bag being placed on a rubber mattress, insulating him from the ground. This suggests that ball lightning can selectively target individuals based on their surroundings and positions.

- **Anomaly 3**: In Yorkshire, during a thunderstorm, Mr. and Mrs. Foster experienced a sudden loss of electricity followed by a loud bang. They discovered significant damage to their television and a physical hole near the socket, indicating a powerful electrical event took place.

- **Anomaly 4**: Rachel Foster felt a thump in her back while near a cooker, coinciding with the appearance of an orange spherical object that was described as moving quickly and silently. This object left a temporary mark on her shoulder before exploding with a loud noise on the wet floor.

- **Anomaly 5**: In Brixham, a fuzzy orange blob resembling a football allegedly came through a wall, causing physical burns to Andrew Clark's foot. The event lasted only about five seconds, suggesting a brief yet intense interaction with the phenomenon.

- **Anomaly 6**: Observations from a storm in Johannesburg in 1920 described a ball of lightning rolling up an incline, complete with irregular streaks and a comet-like tail. This ball exploded upon contact with a wall, leaving no damage behind, demonstrating its strange and dangerous nature. 

- **Anomaly 7**: In Honolulu after a thunderstorm, an unusual flat formation of lightning was observed, wider at its end and brilliantly colored. Its solid appearance and significant dimensions challenged traditional notions of lightning behavior.

- **Anomaly 8**: Reports from Argyllshire in 1975 describe a dome-shaped bulb of lightning that lingered close to the ground with a trailing tail. This phenomenon slowly extinguished without leaving any trace, adding to the enigma of its nature.

- **Anomaly 9**: During a storm, fireballs were seen to fall repeatedly from clouds, with descriptions noting their blunt ends and extended tails. These observations painted a picture of a previously undocumented aspect of lightning.

- **Anomaly 10**: Various sightings in England during the late 19th century recount segments of lightning balls with tails that moved across fields in a wriggling manner. These sightings hint at a complexity of forms associated with ball lightning that deviates from typical depictions of electrical phenomena.
# Similar and Related Phenomena [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Correlation of Solar Activity with Thunderstorm Frequency and Auroras**  
  There is a noted correlation between solar activity and the frequency of thunderstorms, as well as the incidence of auroras. This connection highlights the potential influence of solar phenomena on atmospheric electrical occurrences.

# Examples of the Correlation of Ball Lightning with Solar Activity [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **General Observation of Ball Lightning in Holland**  
  A study from 1880 to 1956 indicated that the annual number of ball lightning reports increased with the intensity of solar activity, measured by Wolf average annual numbers. This suggests a possible relationship between solar cycles and ball lightning occurrences.

# Ball Lightning External to Aircraft (Foo Fighters?) [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Description of Foo Fighters**  
  Foo Fighters are described as fireballs that appear singly or in formation, pacing aircraft despite high relative air speeds. Reports from World War II pilots across various theaters highlight numerous sightings, which have solidified their place in UFO lore, although many occurrences seem to have a tangible basis.

- **Data Evaluation of Foo Fighters**  
  Extensive documentation exists in newspapers and UFO literature, alongside credible records of fireballs accompanying modern aircraft. The puzzling ability of these fireballs to maintain relative positions to fast-moving planes poses significant questions for scientists.

- **Anomaly Evaluation of Foo Fighters**  
  The fundamental anomaly of ball lightning, along with the phenomenon of fireballs maintaining positions relative to high-speed aircraft, presents a considerable scientific puzzle. This behavior cannot easily be dismissed and demands further investigation.

- **Possible Explanation of Foo Fighters**  
  St. Elmo's Fire may account for some observations of fireballs, yet it fails to explain instances where fireballs were situated well away from aircraft surfaces. The lack of definitive explanations continues to make this a subject of intrigue.

# Examples of Ball Lightning External to Aircraft [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **General Observation from Lieutenant Donald Meiers**  
  Lieutenant Meiers identified three types of lights referred to as 'Foo Fighters'; these include a red ball flying alongside the aircraft and vertical rows of fireballs. His descriptions underscore the distinct visual characteristics associated with these phenomena.

- **Incident on May 15, 1970**  
  During a thunderstorm, an aircraft encountered ball lightning while experiencing turbulence and electrical discharges. The presence of a glowing sphere on the wing tip amidst this chaos presents a notable instance of ball lightning interacting with aircraft.

- **No Date or Place Given Incident**  
  An observation recorded a 20 cm ball of light that appeared close to an aircraft's trailing edge while in flight, demonstrating a remarkable ability to maintain a steady course amidst significant airspeed. This raises questions about the physical properties of ball lightning.

- **April 23, 1964, Bedford, England**  
  A loud bang and a flash of light were followed by the appearance of a blue ball of light that vanished after two seconds. This fleeting interaction indicates the unpredictable nature of ball lightning and is emblematic of other sightings.

- **No Date or Place Given with Rapid Movements**  
  A bright ball was observed on an aircraft wing, performing rapid movements unaffected by airflow. This observation contradicts theories that suggest ball lightning might be composed of vaporized metal.

- **Description of St. Elmo's Fire**  
  The occurrence of 'small ball' formations described in conjunction with St. Elmo's Fire included varying diameters and a tendency to roll around the aircraft. Past observations suggest these formations may appear for brief durations, sometimes favoring specific colorations.

- **General Observations of Mysterious Balls of Fire**  
  Allied planes were closely followed by mysterious fireballs, which produced neither explosive nor incendiary effects. This observation reinforces the benign nature of foo fighters compared to the traditional understanding of hostile threats.

- **General Observations from B-29 Pilots**  
  Fireballs consistently followed aircraft during missions in both European and Pacific theaters. B-29 pilots noted their size and phosphorescent glow, alongside their tendency to evade actions to shake them off.

- **December 18, 1944, Germany**  
  Pilots reported sightings of multiple colored lights that tracked their aircraft over Germany. These were described as nuisances rather than threats, adding to the lore of foo fighters during World War II.

- **December 23, 1944, Germany Incident Report**  
  Two lights were observed closely following an aircraft for two minutes, demonstrating an ability to match the altitude and trajectory of manned planes. This incident further supports the existence of these enigmatic phenomena.

- **December 28, 1944, Germany Incident Report**  
  An aircraft encountered lights that appeared to suspend in the air while maneuvering closely to the planes, raising further questions regarding their origin. Despite the closeness of these lights, they went undetected by radar.

- **May 3, 1945, Truk, Oceania**  
  Foo fighters observed in the Pacific parallel to those seen over Germany, challenging the notion that such phenomena were localized to a specific theater of war. This raises intriguing questions about the nature and consistency of foo fighter sightings.

- **General Observations of War-Time Foo Fighters**  
  The relatively low public attention to foo fighters, aside from UFO literature, contrasts with the extensive reports by pilots. Exclusive references in both the New York Times and Herald Tribune highlight the mystery surrounding these encounters.

# Repeating Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Description of Repeating Ball Lightning**  
  This anomaly involves the repeated occurrence of ball lightning in specific geographical locations, often within short timeframes. The phenomenon is not fully understood and elicits questions about what conditions contribute to its recurrence.

- **Data Evaluation of Repeating Ball Lightning**  
  Documented occurrences of this anomaly largely consist of anecdotal evidence, mainly present in British meteorological journals. Evaluating the conditions leading to repeatedly observed ball lightning events remains challenging.

- **Example of Ball Lightning in Virginia**  
  In 1977, a woman was struck by ball lightning in her kitchen during a storm, and astonishingly experienced the same phenomenon again a year later in the same location. She is noted as one of the few individuals recorded to have been struck twice by ball lightning.

- **Example from July 1956, Great Australian Bight**  
  A fishing vessel observed numerous large balls of lightning occurring every few seconds, with a total of over a thousand displays noted. The unusual characteristics of this storm raise questions about the differentiating factors contributing to such a prolific event.

# Penetration of Physical Barriers by Ball Lightning [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Description of Penetration of Barriers**  
  This anomaly describes the phenomenon in which ball lightning penetrates physical barriers like metal screens or glass without necessarily causing damage. Understanding the mechanisms that allow for such penetration adds to the complexity of studying ball lightning.

- **Data Evaluation of Barrier Penetration**  
  There is substantial anecdotal evidence of ball lightning penetrating barriers, but the lack of observed details makes concrete conclusions difficult. Evaluating these events contributes to ongoing discussions on the nature of ball lightning.

- **Example from Lake Victoria, Uganda**  
  During a thunderstorm, several balls of blue light reportedly entered a room through a window and moved within. This account exemplifies the intriguing interactions and behaviors of ball lightning as it relates to enclosed spaces.
# Anomalies Observed in Ball Lightning Phenomena [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **X2. March 21, 1983, Kingsley, North Staffordshire, England.**
  A large white luminous ball entered a factory workshop, blasting through a steel-mesh-reinforced window without fusing the glass. The ball then passed quickly through the processing shop, creating a significant hole in a wall made of 6 mm corrugated asbestos and scattering fragments up to 30 meters away.

- **X3. January 1984, Sochi, U.S.S.R.**
  An Ilyushin-18 encountered a fireball approximately four inches in diameter shortly after takeoff, which appeared on the fuselage and later reemerged in the passenger lounge. The fireball flew around the stunned passengers' heads, eventually splitting into two crescent shapes before noiselessly exiting the aircraft, leaving holes in the fuselage.

- **X4. General observations from Autumn 1992.**
  A compilation of 43 eyewitness accounts documented ball lightning penetrating closed rooms, primarily sourced from the former USSR. These accounts revealed that many balls entered through closed glass windows, with some instances showing no damage, while others presented neat circular holes as though melted or punched through the glass.

- **X5. July 27, 1952, Hamburg, Germany.**
  A witness observed a luminous ball the size of a fist that moved through the glass of a closed window without causing any damage. The ball traveled a meter into the room, made a sharp turn, and disappeared after a loud blast, leaving behind an odor typical of electric discharges.

- **X6. No date given, New South Wales, Australia.**
  Among 5315 descriptions of ball-lightning events collected by D.J. Turner, there were instances where balls bored holes in glass window panes or passed through them as if no barrier existed. This led to speculation about electromagnetic induction, raising questions about how ball lightning can traverse solid glass without damage.
# Mountain-Top Glows (GLD1) [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Mountain-top glows** are characterized by rays, undulating streamers of light, flashes, and steady glows appearing along mountain crests and ridges. These displays usually exhibit a color spectrum dominated by yellowish white, green, and orange, and have been observed in regions such as the Andes, Alps, and Rockies.

- Scientifically, mountain-top glows have received little attention, and data evaluation shows that instrumented studies are essentially unknown. Despite this, there are many high-quality observations, particularly from the Andes and Alps.

- Anomalous aspects include the very large scale of the phenomenon, heightened displays during earthquakes, possible periodicity of flashes, and resemblance to auroras, which suggests a reality of low-level auroras. 

# Intermountain Electric Discharges (GLD2) [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Intermountain electric discharges** refer to luminous discharges or lightning observed between mountain peaks, typically accompanied by sound akin to thunder. Testimonies and anecdotes primarily back these claims, although some of this evidence is questionable regarding its scientific validity.

- The phenomenon remains elusive, with ball lightning noted as a possible explanation, but the actual conditions needed for its presence remain unclear. Local geological factors, such as the presence of ore bodies, may contribute to these discharges, but confirmation through scientific observation is lacking.

# Large-Scale St. Elmo’s Fire (GLD3) [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Large-scale St. Elmo’s Fire** manifests as the enveloping of people, buildings, and landscapes by transient, flame-like electrical discharges, often preceded by thunder. Individuals surrounded by this phenomenon do not experience physical harm, though they may encounter minor electrical sensations and breathing difficulties.

- This phenomenon fills a niche between regular St. Elmo's Fire and brush discharge, yet is primarily supported by old reports, leading to a lower confidence rating. The consensus suggests that strong potential gradients during electrical storms are the primary cause, despite the extensive areas involved.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly X1 (1837, Orkney)**  
  The phenomenon involved a blood-red flame extending along the shore, surprised observers by its brilliance and the four occurrences of flame in quick succession. It was connected to an iron chain and appeared to float above the water without contact.

- **Anomaly X2 (Circa 1842, Perthshire)**  
  Two men found themselves engulfed in flames seemingly emerging from the heather they traversed, with the fire adhering to them despite attempts to extinguish it. This terrifying experience led the witnesses to believe they narrowly escaped death by fire.

- **Anomaly X3 (August 5, 1879, Jura Mountains)**  
  As a storm approached, a pine forest was suddenly illuminated by a light resembling tropical sea phosphorescence, which fluctuated with the sound of thunder. This strange light intensified with every thunderclap, providing an eerily beautiful spectacle until the storm subsided.

- **Anomaly X4 (June 1880, Clarens)**  
  During a thunderstorm, a cherry tree was struck by lightning, causing an electric field to envelope a nearby girl gathering cherries. Remarkably, neither the girl nor the cemetery's occupants, who also experienced electrical sensations, suffered any injuries.

- **Anomaly X5 (August 12, 1884, Scotland)**  
  A man reported being enveloped in a luminous light that felt like illuminated steam, causing a discomfort he described as indescribable. Observers nearby confirmed seeing the strange cloud surrounding him, adding to its mystery.

- **Anomaly X6 (February 10, 1888, Off the English coast)**  
  A ship reported being enveloped in bright red flames during a thunderstorm, while the thunder and lightning remained distanced from the vessel. The crew described the event as extraordinarily violent and not resembling any typical lightning flash.

- **Anomaly X7 (November 8, 1930, New Zealand)**  
  During a thunderstorm, an entire house was surrounded by blue flickering flames that lasted for a significant period. Along with this, three horses in the vicinity were also seen engulfed in the same blue flames.

- **Anomaly X8 (May 30, 1869, Greiffenberg)**  
  People caught outside in a thunderstorm found themselves seemingly wrapped in flames that deprived them of air. This bizarre and alarming experience left observers baffled regarding the nature of the fire.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly X4**: Long sparks were observed rising from the gypsum dunes in White Sands National Monument during a thunderstorm. These sparks extended straight up from the tops of several dunes, with no branches observed, indicating a unique electrical activity under specific weather conditions.

- **Anomaly X5**: During electrified dust storms in Kansas, no thunder or lightning is observed, and metallic objects insulated from the earth become highly electrified. Observations include ranchers noticing glowing lights on the tips of their cattle's horns, leading to confusion regarding the effects of these phenomena.

- **Anomaly X6**: On January 18, 1817, during a rapid fall of wet snow in Vermont, people reported being enveloped by light, accompanied by significant St. Elmo's Fire occurrences on fence posts and horses. This unusual display of light was a notable example of atmospheric electricity interacting with snow conditions.

- **Anomaly X7**: On January 20, 1895, flashes of light were observed during a dust storm in Oklahoma. This event suggests that atmospheric disturbances can produce visual electrical phenomena even without the typical indicators of thunder or lightning.

- **Anomaly X8**: Purple and milky-white flashes were documented during a snowstorm in Geneva, Ohio, on November 19, 1903. The presence of faint rolling thunder added to the unusual atmospheric conditions contributing to these visual anomalies.

- **Anomaly X9**: In Lake Maracaibo, Venezuela, constant silent lightning was reported, particularly visible near the bar at the southern end of the lake. Known as the Faro of Maracaibo since the Spanish Conquest, this phenomenon represents a fascinating aspect of continuous electrical activity in a specific geographic area.

- **Anomaly X10**: Observations of blue sparks attributed to wind-driven sand were recorded in Khartum, Sudan, and Kano, Nigeria. These occurrences highlight the interaction of particles in the atmosphere with electrical phenomena during dust storms.

- **Anomaly X1**: On June 7, 1882, St. Elmo's Fire was observed on Pike's Peak, where a researcher experienced luminous effects on his body in the presence of atmospheric electrical excitement. The display included fingers igniting with light, adding intrigue to the natural electrical phenomena associated with storms.

- **Anomaly X2**: A unique display of fireballs was noted while a farmer drove along a private road bordered by wire fences. These fireballs, traveling along the wires and emitting crackling sounds, caused disturbances without producing significant noise apart from the crackling.

- **Anomaly X3**: In Worcester, Massachusetts, a man saw a ball of light moving along a railroad track while walking. The ball's slow movement and its eventual disappearance were accompanied by a clap of thunder, raising questions about electrical behaviors in unusual conditions.

- **Anomaly X4**: In Hilversum, Holland on March 7, 1932, luminous spheres appeared on a broadcasting antenna when power was increased. These moving spheres created an impressive visual effect, resembling a string of beads following the antenna wires.

- **Anomaly X5**: In January 1924, six incandescent disks or globes illuminated a barn roof in a manner that attracted observers' attention. The phenomena included disks falling at regular intervals while maintaining their brilliance, creating an intriguing light display.

- **Anomaly X6**: A light resembling a small flame was observed revolving around the third pyramid in Giza, Egypt. This light made several circuits around the pyramid, highlighting curiosities associated with ancient structures and atmospheric phenomena.

- **Anomaly X7**: Sparks resembling fire were reported on C-street in Virginia City, Nevada, appearing to explode when coming into contact with water pools beneath telegraph wires. This unusual display suggested a connection between electromagnetic activity and environmental moisture.

- **Anomaly X8**: On August 18, 1876, small luminous columns were reported descending obliquely onto writing paper amidst loud thunder. This phenomenon left behind a slight noise, paralleling the ambient electrical activity associated with thunderstorms.

- **Anomaly X9**: In January 1748, a unique electrical phenomenon was noted when a cat's fur was rubbed, producing sparks resembling those from a comb. The resulting balls of fire danced around, leading to a surprising interaction with sound and light.

- **Anomaly X10**: A violent storm on February 18, 1877, in the North Atlantic Ocean resulted in large balls of phosphorescent fire appearing on the ship. These luminous displays created the appearance of the vessel being adorned with lights, exemplifying oceanic electrical phenomena.

- **Anomaly X11**: On December 3, 1894, the British ship Mercedes witnessed balls of electric fire exploding and darting among masts during a storm on the Grand Banks. This spectacular display lasted two nights, illuminating the surroundings and allowing for temporary repairs under its eerie glow.

- **Anomaly X12**: In the Strait of Malacca on April 17, 1994, sudden electrostatic discharges were observed along the ship's bridge wing. The luminous activity and sounds associated with the discharge provided a real-time example of electrical phenomena in maritime conditions.

- **Anomaly X13**: On August 20, 1996, vivid luminous and acoustic phenomena were reported while aboard the m.v. Eastern Bridge in the North Atlantic Ocean. Observers noted that movement caused flames to emanate from fingers, capturing the essence of fleeting electrical displays.

- **Anomaly X14**: A contagious example of St. Elmo's fire was recorded on February 8, 1999, in the North Sea, where observers experienced short flames appearing at their fingertips. The shared electric sensations created remarkable interactions in the absence of electrical storms.

- **Anomaly X15**: On May 8, 1998, during a violent lightning storm, green St. Elmo's fire was noted glowing on the aerials of a ship. Accompanied by distinct crackling sounds, this electrical manifestation illustrated the dynamic interplay of weather and atmospheric electricity.
# Curiosities of St. Elmo’s Fire [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Corposant Phenomenon**  
The corposant's six-fold symmetry resembles that of snowflakes, suggesting a speculative connection between these phenomena. It's believed that electrical forces may control the symmetry of both snowflakes and corposants.

- **Insect Antennae Illumination**  
In an experiment by physicist P. Callahan, insects placed in an electric field had their antennae illuminated, resembling St. Elmo's fire. This suggests that swarms of migrating insects might produce strange luminous effects during electrical storms.

- **Zeitoun Phenomenon**  
The Zeitoun phenomenon, associated with luminous electrical discharge, often occurs near church steeples during storms. Some observers attribute its luminosities to mystical sources, while others suggest a link to seismic events.

- **Association of Luminous Phenomena and Earthquakes**  
Data indicates an increase in luminous phenomena in Zeitoun correlates with heightened seismic activity. This supports the hypothesis that luminous phenomena can be associated with tectonic strain in the Earth's crust.

# Luminous Aerial Bubbles [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Biskopsberga, Sweden (1808)**  
Observers reported numerous spherical bodies drifting across the sky, appearing dark brown until they neared the sun. As they approached the ground, they transformed into colorful, invisible bubbles before vanishing.

- **Remenham, England (January 1871)**  
Colored air bubbles, reminiscent of rubber balls, rose and floated in a rising current from the snow, reflecting colors in the room. This appearance lasted about two minutes before the bubbles moved away.

- **Ringstead Bay, England (August 1876)**  
Participants saw luminous spheres, like billiard balls, floating and moving up and down around them. The sight presented a vivid, iridescent display without any accompanying noise, mesmerizing the observers.

- **Trakehnen, Germany (March 22, 1880)**  
A remarkable phenomenon unfolded with luminous bodies rising in a horizontal direction, some resembling sparks. They moved as a connected series, shining brightly as they traversed the sky.

- **Glenora, New York (circa 1882)**  
In a thunderstorm, numerous luminescent balls were observed bouncing about, appearing static at times but then springing back into the air. They resembled static electricity and created a buoyant spectacle in the air.

- **Cherry Valley, New York (April 1886)**  
Two men described the floating spherical bodies, resembling soap bubbles, seen descending among apple trees. They appeared in various colors and vanished as they approached the ground, leaving behind an eerie recollection.

- **Kailana, Himalayas (June 1898)**  
During a misty evening post-thunderstorm, pale round lights floated in the mist, distinct from fireflies despite their similar glow. The observers monitored the lights until they faded from sight.

- **Salisbury, England (December 18, 1922)**  
An extraordinary display of bluish, misty flame balls occurred, either hovering or darting irregularly against the backdrop of lightning. This phenomenon lasted for at least two hours and mesmerized local observers.

- **Havana, Cuba (May 15, 1836)**  
During a solar eclipse, numerous luminous globules detached from the sun and moved across the sky in various directions, contributing to the strange atmospheric conditions observed.

- **Venice, Italy (May 21, 1877)**  
Witnesses reported seeing fireballs rising from a dark cloud and bursting, with behavior reminiscent of soap bubbles. This dazzling display added to the intrigue of the atmospheric phenomena experienced at that time.

- **Wiltshire, England (November 15, 1899)**  
Reports from this day describe an air filled with small luminous bodies, creating an enchanting yet mysterious atmosphere. This event left a mark on the observers, stirring much curiosity.

- **Devonport, England (May 10, 1902)**  
At sunset, observers noted the sky filled with brilliantly colored "suns" that bore a strong resemblance to toy balloons. The sight added a whimsical quality to the late afternoon sky.

- **New York, New York (September 21, 1910)**  
A multitude of round objects traversed the sky in an orderly west-east pattern, initially mistaken for balloons. The observation contributed to the growing catalog of unexplained aerial phenomena.

- **South Atlantic Ocean (September 1943)**  
An observer noted a multicolored object, about the size of a basketball, moving slowly across the water during a voyage. Its appearance, characterized by its membranous structure, intrigued the ship's crew.
# Strange Bubble Phenomenon [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- In August 1984, H. Curtis witnessed a tennis ball-sized bubble in Winchester, England, which sailed horizontally without disintegrating. This event was puzzling, as it seemed to reflect the tarmac road, despite the fact that bubble mixtures do not produce such discernible reflections.

- The bubble continued to travel at a consistent speed before ascending towards a six-foot wall, eventually drifting out of sight into a public park. Curtis found it incredible that the bubble remained intact for such a duration.

# Mekong River Lights [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- In October 1994, in the Mekong River near Nongkhai, Thailand, observers reported seeing lights rising from the river's bottom, which then shot into the sky like missiles. Local lore attributed the phenomenon to a serpent releasing her eggs, and it was said to occur annually during the full moon.

- A. Pentecost related these occurrences to the will-o'-the-wisp, traditionally explained by marsh gas igniting. However, the lights originate underwater, which raises questions about this explanation and led to hypotheses involving phosphorescent bacteria or phosphorus vapor.

# Earthquake Lights [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- Earthquake lights manifest as various luminous phenomena, such as flashes in the sky, ball lightning, and aurora-like rays, seen near earthquakes. They can occur before, during, or after seismic events and have been recorded throughout history despite doubts about their legitimacy by some scientists.

- Observations of earthquake lights have shown extensive variety, many of which may be misattributed to commonplace electrical disturbances or psychological effects stemming from earthquakes. Evidence persists that suggests an underlying electrical phenomenon may contribute to their occurrence.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **No date given. Derby, England.**  
  A black aurora was observed at the time of the quake, which is referenced as GLA22. This unusual phenomenon indicates a possible connection between seismic activity and atmospheric anomalies.

- **April 13, 1750. England.**  
  Red rays were seen converging at the zenith during this period. The sighting adds to the catalog of strange celestial events associated with earthquakes.

- **April 15, 1752. Stavanger, Norway.**  
  An octagonal luminosity in the sky emitted fireballs from its angles. This phenomenon highlights the extraordinary nature of atmospheric reactions possibly linked to tectonic activity.

- **February 1692. Sicily.**  
  During an episode of earthquakes, the entire village of Alari appeared to be in flames for about 6 minutes. This striking visual experience raises questions about the interactions between seismic events and environmental conditions.

- **December 30, 1820. Ionian Isles, Greece.**  
  A fireball measuring 4-6 feet was visible over the sea for 5 or 6 minutes shortly before the earthquake shock. This report of a luminous object preceding seismic disruption is notable for its potential implications regarding earthquake prediction.

- **1738. Pecs, Hungary.**  
  A black cliff named Szennyes emitted fire for three days, coinciding with a strongly felt earthquake. The event is described as neither volcanic nor easily explainable, deepening the mystery of such phenomena associated with seismic activity.

- **June 26, 1830. Honan Province, China.**  
  Days prior to the quake, burning vapors filled the atmosphere, along with 194 dull explosions heard in the air. Long bands of fire appeared on the horizon, suggesting a precursor phenomenon related to seismic events.

- **September 30, 1924. Portland, Maine.**  
  A brilliant flare of light was observed across the clouded sky at the moment of seismic shock. The event was scrutinized by astronomers, who dismissed meteorological explanations, adding to the intrigue of earthquake-related luminosity.

- **May 1960. Arauco Peninsula, Chile.**  
  Residents reported luminous phenomena during the earthquakes, with particularly vivid descriptions from a pilot observing aftershocks. The lights, described as a bluish-green color, were seen alongside the tremors, suggesting a significant correlation.

- **Winter 1988-1989. Quebec, Canada.**  
  Numerous reports of earthquake lights were recorded alongside 54 seismic shocks. Observers reported a variety of luminous phenomena, contributing to a better understanding of earthquake lights and their manifestations.

- **January 17, 1995. Kobe, Japan.**  
  Various residents witnessed aurora-like phenomena and flashes of light coinciding with the earthquake. Geological evidence suggests that these lights may originate from electrical discharges related to the seismic activity.

- **September 16, 1931. Yamanasi Prefecture, Japan.**  
  Intense luminosity was reported during the Yamanasi earthquake, with a wide variety of luminous phenomena recorded. The diversity of observed lights points to multiple potential mechanisms involved in the generation of such phenomena.
# Volcano Lights [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **April 7, 1906 - Mount Vesuvius, Italy**  
  During a vigorous eruption, the frequency of explosions ranged from one every three to three per second. Each explosion was accompanied by a thin, luminous arc that flashed upward and outward from the crater before the explosion could be sensed.

- **August 4, 1979 - Mount Etna, Italy**  
  Scientists reported strange bolts of bright light shooting out of the crater during the eruption. This phenomenon, likened to lightning, was said to be caused by electrical activity with the eruption, marking an unprecedented occurrence.

- **1902 - Mount Misery, Leeward Islands**  
  A loud explosion was heard by laborers working on the side of Mount Misery, accompanied by flames leaping out of the ground. At the same time, a heavy thunderstorm rolled in with vivid lightning flashes.

- **1902 - Mont Pelee, Martinique**  
  At 11 p.m., lightning shot from the mountain in all directions, zigzagging, accompanied by reddish globes that ascended and exploded. Another focus of electrical energy was observed about forty miles south-west of the volcano, suggesting an atmospheric connection between the two active centers.

# Tornado Lights [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **December 12, 1846 - St. Just, England**  
  During the passage of powerful whirlwinds, a vivid pillar of fire rose from the sea, illuminating the tornado's center. The phenomenon rendered the electric fluid's passage from the earth to the clouds visible through constant lightning flashes.

- **June 22, 1928 - Greensburg, Kansas**  
  As the tornado rose, a remarkable searchlight beam was observed extending from the cloud to the skyline. The beam appeared bright with sharp edges, indicating strong directional luminosity before the funnel formed.

- **May 25, 1955 - Blackwell, Oklahoma**  
  Eyewitnesses reported intense electrical activity, describing a light resembling a bright electric arc welder. Observations included a luminescent band of light amidst rapidly rotating clouds in the tornado.

- **May 31, 1956 - No place given**  
  An odd flashing patch of illumination appeared in the top section of a thundercloud, characterized by a circular patch of light. This phenomenon persisted in an on-and-off pattern without any visible lightning activity.

- **June 17, 1959 - Miami, Florida**  
  The lower part of the tornado was constantly illuminated with a blue-green light, giving the appearance of an electric welding torch. This illumination was attributed partly to tornado disruptions of power lines in the area.

- **April 11, 1965 - Toledo, Ohio**  
  The tornado exhibited a beautiful electric blue light accompanied by orange balls of lightning. Observers described a surface glow of rolling noise along with luminous, round shapes darting around in the clouds above.

- **April 3, 1974 - Huntsville, Alabama**  
  A green spike of light was witnessed, which moved and burst like a bombing effect. Observers saw a bright blue flash resembling an electric arc, leading them to believe it was caused by a power line shorting out.

- **General observations - Tornado funnel**  
  A tornado funnel was reported to turn white suddenly, emitting light of its own rather than fog. The accompanying electricity caused radiant blue light and rolling surface glows up to several feet deep.

- **1885 - Greene County, Alabama**  
  A phosphorescent glow resembling a sunrise was reported just before a tornado's approach. The illumination was striking enough to be notable among witnesses.

- **October 17, 1909 - Mediterranean Sea**  
  A streak of light along the axis of a waterspout was reported, occurring alongside vivid lightning. This spectacle was indicative of the extraordinary electrical activity of the weather phenomenon.

- **March 23, 1913 - Omaha, Nebraska**  
  Observers reported the lower end of the tornado appearing as a lurid, fiery mass. This fierce light emanation was noted as a significant aspect of the tornado's physical presence.

- **August 21, 1933 - Orkneys, England**  
  After a tornado passed, a strange bright object was seen in the sky, raising questions about the phenomenon's connection to prior electrical discharges. The sight left a lasting impression on those who witnessed it.

- **February 10, 1959 - St. Louis, Missouri**  
  A blue flash of light and a big ball of fire in the sky accompanied a strong glow of illumination. Eyewitness accounts detailed the impressive nature of the event.

- **March 26, 1968 - Monroe, Michigan**  
  A tornado was observed alongside a beautiful blue light, contributing to the eerie visual atmosphere of the event. The distinct illumination became one of the memorable features of the storm.

- **May 15, 1968 - Tuckerman, Arkansas**  
  Inside the tornado funnel, a pale-green, neon-like light was reported. This radiant quality added to the unusual characteristics of the tornado.

- **July 1890 - Fort Bowie, Arizona**  
  A curious anecdote described a tornado that revolved and threw off immense volumes of white vapor. Witnesses noted incessant and blinding lightning that played around the wheels of passing vehicles.

- **August 30, 1971 - No place given**  
  A spectator observed a lightning discharge throughout the length of a narrow tornado core high in the atmosphere. The unusual brightness of the core provided a stark contrast to the surrounding environment.

- **June 21, 1972 - Tempe, Arizona**  
  Electrical discharges were clearly observed at the base of a nearby tornado, contributing to the air’s charged atmosphere. These discharges hinted at significant electrical activity connected to the tornado.

- **August 18, 1890 - Brittany, France**  
  A tornado devastated a significant area while accompanied by incessant lightning strikes. Witnesses reported flashes of violet flames that erupted from the ground amidst the tornado's trajectory.

- **May 3, 1943 - McKinney, Texas**  
  A survivor described a peculiar bluish light and vapor-like streamers inside the tornado funnel. The unique observations detailed an otherworldly view of the tornado's interior.

- **July 21, 1992 - Hill Deverill, Wiltshire, England**  
  A couple reported potentially luminous whirlwinds while driving home, indicating extraordinary meteorological phenomena. Such sightings evoke the mysterious intersections of tornadoes and electrical displays.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly X23: Tornado Encounter**  
  D. McGown witnessed a massive, twisting funnel while fishing, described as spinning rapidly outside but lazily inside. The tornado exhibited tiny fingers of lightning and passed over him, causing destruction but leaving the luminous activity unexamined.

- **Anomaly X24: Mysterious Illumination in Surrey**  
  M.D. Smith observed an orange glow outside his window accompanied by a roaring sound, which occurred multiple times. The second instance revealed a narrow column of silvery-golden illumination that moved slowly towards the ground, lasting five to six seconds.

- **Anomaly X25: Possible Explanation for Tornado Luminosity**  
  Electrical discharges inside tornadoes are common explanations for observed luminosities, alongside the theory that tornadoes might pick up combustible materials. The possibility of tornado luminosity being linked to ball lightning has also been suggested.

- **Anomaly X1: Waterspout in France (1823)**  
  A blackish vapor waterspout was observed, flames sometimes visible, and it caused substantial destruction to trees and homes. This phenomenon violently broke apart buildings in the village of Marchepoy.

- **Anomaly X2: Fiery Cloud in Carcassonne**  
  A thick black cloud appeared over a field, accompanied by crackling sounds and an enormous column of fire that damaged property and livestock. Witnesses reported a strong sulfurous odor during the event.

- **Anomaly X3: Pillars of Fire in Lancashire**  
  Counted three fiery pillars rising from the earth, displaying significant height and circumference. Witnesses described the extraordinary appearance of these fiery manifestations while travelling in a phaeton.

- **Anomaly X4: Flaming Whirlwind in Tennessee (1869)**  
  A whirlwind developed, carrying burning tree branches and igniting buildings in its path. The strong winds and ensuing inferno were witnessed by not less than 200 people, all sharing similar descriptions.

- **Anomaly X5: Pillar of Fire over Carlisle (1869)**  
  A visible pillar of fire passed over Carlisle, recognized for its unusual heat and size. This spectacle exploded in the air, lighting the surroundings and startling the onlookers.

- **Anomaly X6: Ball of Fire in Banbury (1872)**  
  Witness accounts describe a ball of fire traveling low to the ground, creating a loud roaring sound and causing destruction in its wake. Various witnesses reported similar visual and sonic experiences.

- **Anomaly X7: Whirlwind and Fire in Georgia (1881)**  
  A small whirlwind formed over a cornfield, tearing up stalks and emitting strange vapors. Witnesses described the accompanying sounds and the burning atmosphere affecting their health from a distance.

- **Anomaly X8: Column of Water with Fire in India (1928)**  
  A cylindrical column of water was reported to emit fire and noise, leaving a deep hole at the paddy-field and creating significant disturbances in the area. Witnesses observed the destruction caused by this phenomenon.

- **Anomaly X9: Strange Noise and Fire in Spain (1934)**  
  A black cloud appeared accompanied by an overwhelming fire, leading to the destruction of a hut and surrounding area. The sounds and aftermath were shocking to those present, with significant adverse effects observed.
# Anomaly Evaluation [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Luminous Rocks and Other Solid Materials (X1)**
  The appearance of luminous emanations has been documented at sites above extensive ore deposits since at least 1747, especially before or during thunderstorms. While some reports may stem from superstition or observational errors, recent investigations have confirmed these phenomena and suggest they are linked to electric emissions in conductive areas.

- **Rock Luminosity Photographic Experiments (X2)**
  Photographic experiments in the Isle of Wight sought to explore whether energy is transmitted instantaneously to distant regions during earthquakes. Results indicated that certain weeks showed no photographic effects, while others revealed bands of darkness that suggested a feeble light emission from chalk surfaces.

- **Intense Electrical Storm in Switzerland (X3)**
  During a severe electrical storm in Switzerland in 1875, an unusual phosphorescence was noted in various elements, including the ground and hailstones. This phenomenon was accompanied by a noticeable increase in ozone, leading observers to liken the scent to garlic.

- **Ski Runners' Observation (X4)**
