  The object was well-defined and well-lit but briefly obscured by clouds. It dipped and bobbled in its trajectory before flying into a storm with baseball-sized hail, then reemerged apparently undamaged.

- Anomaly: Pulsating light observed aboard the s.t.s. Astrid  
At 0443 UTC, a light resembling a satellite was sighted above the ship for about 10-15 seconds, moving west to northwest with a pulsating white light. The light stopped suddenly, turned towards the ship, illuminated the area briefly like a spotlight, then moved very fast and vanished below the horizon.

- Anomaly: Erratic motion of the light  
The light's erratic motion and use of a searchlight resemble reports of UFOs in popular publications. However, the Marine Observer's report provides sound scientific credentials, suggesting the phenomenon is noteworthy beyond typical explanations.
# Moving Phosphorescent Bands GLW2 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly Description:** Long, parallel bars or ripples of light moving swiftly across the sea, usually at speeds of 60-100 miles per hour. The bands are generally 10-30 feet wide and can extend from a few feet to several miles in length, with significant variation in their dimensions.

- **Anomaly Evaluation:** The exact stimulus and mechanism for generating these luminous bands are currently unknown. It has been rated a 2 due to the lack of understanding in how the bands maintain their geometry and apparent motion.

- **Possible Explanations:** The luminous bands are assumed to be caused by bioluminescent organisms, although some skepticism exists. The perceived speed of the bands may be an illusion created by sequentially pulsing groups of luminous organisms, possibly triggered by stimuli like ship's radar or engine noise.

- **Similar and Related Phenomena:** Related phenomena include phosphorescent wheels and synchronously flashing fireflies. Additionally, luminous patches at ground level and luminous fogs may present similar wave-like structures.

- **Example X1:** On April 11, 1870, in the Gulf of Siam, observers reported long white waves of light that increased in brightness before vanishing, creating a fog-like appearance. The phenomenon lasted about five minutes before once more reappearing briefly.

- **Example X2:** In 1883 in Greenland waters, a steady yellowish band of light was observed approaching the ship, creating the illusion of steaming through a sea of fire as it passed. This light was distinct from the bluish-white phosphorescent light typically seen in the ship's wake.

- **Example X3:** Circa 1906 in the Gulf of Oman, shafts of brilliant light swept across the ship's bow at extraordinary speeds, resembling large guns firing rectangular bars of light. After an initial display, the phenomenon returned moving in the opposite direction, creating an enchanting effect.

- **Example X4:** On August 8, 1950, in the Arabian Sea, brightly flashing patches of phosphorescence covered the sea surface, appearing as circles and oblongs. These patches shimmered and flickered intermittently, giving the sea a glistening appearance.

- **Example X5:** On October 23, 1954, in the Java Sea, closely spaced patches of phosphorescence were observed around the ship that flashed in regular intervals. The illumination appeared to evolve from irregular patterns to clearly defined bands of light.

- **Example X6:** On September 27, 1959, in the East Indian Archipelago, parallel beams of light were observed, mistaken initially for fishing boats' flashlights. These beams were synchronized, creating a surreal landscape resembling boiling seas around the ship. 

- **Example X7:** In April 1875 in the Gulf of Mexico, observers noted a series of luminous pulsations that swept past their ship. This occurrence reflects the ongoing mystery of phosphorescent phenomena at sea.

- **Example X8:** In April 1901 in the Persian Gulf, two luminous bands were seen moving at about 60 miles per minute, initially in one direction and then reversing. This rapid alternation showcases the dynamic nature of these phenomena. 

- **Example X9:** In April 1901, another observation in the Persian Gulf reported bands that were 50 feet wide, similar to the previous entry. Their consistent width and movement added to the bewildering experiences reported by mariners.

- **Example X10:** Flashes of light passing over the sea were recorded in 1905 in the Indian Ocean, contributing to the growing body of evidence of extraordinary sighting experiences at sea. 

- **Example X11:** In October 1924, bands were observed passing at the rate of two per second while changing direction frequently. This demonstrates the complexities and variability of luminescent displays on the sea.

- **Example X12:** On March 21, 1925, off the west coast of India, bands were spaced about one second apart, followed by the appearance of an anticlockwise light wheel. This anecdote illustrates the association between moving bands and rotating light displays.

- **Example X13:** In November 1926, bands measuring 5 feet wide, were recorded in the Gulf of Siam, creating an extensive and striking visual experience for those aboard. 

- **Example X14:** In November 1928 in the China Sea, three waves of light per second were reported, creating a spectacle that changed direction after a period. 

- **Example X15:** Another manifestation of luminescent phenomena occurred in November 1930 in the Gulf of Oman, demonstrating the historical consistency of these sightings. 

- **Example X16:** On May 21, 1936, in the Gulf of Siam, two bands were observed per second over a duration of 30 minutes, showcasing sustained luminous activity typically observed at sea. 

- **Example X17:** In November 1953, in the China Sea, long, straight, greyish bands were noted, passing at three per second for 15 minutes, before fading to darkness. 

- **Example X18:** On April 2, 1954, similar luminous waves were observed in the Red Sea, continuing the remarkable documentation of light phenomena in different geographical locations. 

- **Example X19:** In May 1955, a phenomenon was recorded in the Gulf of Siam, contributing to the rich tapestry of maritime luminescence observations over the years. 

- **Example X20:** On April 2, 1956, waves of light in the Persian Gulf were detected, coinciding with echometer readings of shoals of fish, adding a dimension of ecological connection to the sightings. 

- **Example X21:** In October 1961 in the China Sea, noted phenomena continue to affirm the historical regularity of these striking luminescent occurrences. 

- **Example X22:** On April 22, 1971, pale-green bands approximately 2 miles long were observed in the Malacca Strait moving at a speed of 24 knots, exemplifying the thrilling nature of these experiences. 

- **Example X23:** On December 8, 1974, in the Arabian Sea, green bands measuring 50 by 20 meters were noted, accompanied by many bioluminescent organisms.  

- **Example X24:** An unidentified date and location reported sixty bands per minute, yielding a mesmerizing spectacle with two rotating light wheels moving in opposing directions. 

- **Example X25:** On April 23, 1955, a medley of moving bands, wheels, and expanding circles was recorded in the Persian Gulf, showcasing the multifaceted nature of marine luminescent phenomena. 

- **Example X26:** Waves of light recorded in May 1955 in the Gulf of Aden, with heights of 12 feet above the sea, reflect the extensive nature of bioluminescent displays. 

- **Example X27:** Moving bars of luminous mist observed above the water surface in January 1880 off the Malabar Coast indicate the historical context of these marine phenomena. 

- **Example X28:** In May 1906, a transition from moving parallel bands to a wheel display was documented near Bender Abbas in the Indian Ocean, indicating a potential link between these various light phenomena. 

- **Example X29:** In April 1953, another occurrence in the Arabian Sea followed a similar transformation from parallel bands to a wheel display, reinforcing the recurring nature of these observations. 

- **Example X30:** Reports of displays alternating between moving bands and wheels were noted in September 1955 in the Gulf of Aden, accentuating the variability and dynamism of luminescent phenomena.

- **Example X31:** A change from moving bands to wheel displays recorded in April 1956 in the Persian Gulf further highlights the connections between various types of light phenomena experienced at sea. 

- **Example X32:** In May 1963, a display of moving bands changing into two rotating wheels in the Persian Gulf, offers additional insight into the complex nature of maritime luminescence. 

- **Example X33:** Events from May 30, 1956, in the Persian Gulf remain part of the extensive chronicles of such marine marvels. 

- **Example X34:** On March 27, 1976, a moving band system transitioning into a wheel display was reported in the Gulf of Siam, reinforcing witnessed transitions between different luminescent states. 

- **Example X35:** Another entry on June 12, 1980, highlights continued observations of maritime luminescence in the South China Sea, indicative of an enduring phenomenon.

- **Example X36:** Recollection of the April 11, 1870 sightings in the Gulf of Siam reinforces the historical anecdote of phosphorescent activity at sea.

- **Example X37:** A similar observation from July 23, 1954, in the Indian Ocean, indicated pale white light shafts moving swiftly above the surface, demonstrating consistency in performances of marine luminescence.

- **Example X38:** Rapidly moving luminous bands observed in July 1938 across the Indian Ocean offer further evidence supporting the geographical fluctuation of these phenomena.

- **Example X39:** A strange dawn-like light reported on April 23, 1963, during a voyage from Singapore to Japan, uniquely describes the nature of maritime luminescence encountered. 

- **Example X40:** On May 29, 1985, the bioluminescent display observed near the Java Sea indicates the continuing relevance and fascination with such phenomena, connecting to the historical documentations.
# Aerial Phosphorescent Displays [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Display Description**: Marine phosphorescent displays observed in the air well above the water's surface appear as luminous phenomena. These displays are believed to be caused by bioluminescent organisms, though some situations create uncertainty over whether the display is on the surface or just above it.

- **Data Evaluation**: A limited number of credible cases have been reported, holding a rating of 2. The concern lies in the lack of recognized light sources for the aerial phosphorescent displays, as bioluminescent organisms are not known to remain in the atmosphere for extended periods.

- **Possible Explanations**: While the prolonged suspension of bioluminescent organisms in the air is conceivable, it is deemed unlikely. The probability that the displays are light reflections from mist or fog, influenced by underwater light sources, is also considered, although some observers reject this explanation.

- **Similar and Related Phenomena**: The displays have similarities with low-level auroras and luminous fogs, which are intriguing due to their wave structures.

# Phosphorescent Wheels [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Display Characteristics**: Phosphorescent wheels are characterized by spoke-like bands of light rotating around a center that can change rotational direction. The spokes may vary in shape, and the displays can be stationary or in motion, often observed in Indian Ocean regions.

- **Data Evaluation**: Numerous well-documented accounts exist, warranting a strong reliability rating of 1. However, the fundamental concern regarding the origin and continuity of these geometric light patterns remains unexplained, leading to an anomaly rating of 2.

- **Possible Explanations**: Bioluminescence is presumed to be the light source, yet there are instances where ship at wakes are not luminescent when the wheel displays are active. Earthquake activity is suggested as a potential trigger for bioluminescent phenomena, but the sustained intricate geometries challenge this hypothesis.

- **Similar and Related Phenomena**: The wheels are linked to other marine phosphorescent displays and low-level auroras, presenting an area for further investigation regarding electrical origins. 

- **Imagery of Observation**: The phenomenon has been vividly described through various historical accounts, often highlighting their breathtaking and awe-inspiring nature, with reports of wheels appearing both above the water and in unique configurations that strengthen interest in their study.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Anomaly X16:** On September 9, 1929, in the Malacca Strait, bands of light were seen wheeling over the sea for a duration of 1 hour. The display was combined with vivid lightning, adding to the spectacle.

- **Anomaly X17:** On December 28, 1929, near an S-shaped spoke passing a ship in the North Indian Ocean, the hub was approximately 5 miles away. During this display, the ship's engines were straining, indicating some kind of unusual activity.

- **Anomaly X22:** On April 16, 1950, in the Gulf of Siam, multiple wheels were observed simultaneously for a duration of 30 minutes. This unusual phenomenon elicited curiosity among onlookers as the wheels moved in a synchronized manner.

- **Anomaly X23:** On April 5, 1953, in the Arabian Sea, shafts of pale white diffused light appeared on the water, traveling at great speed. The shafts then formed into a rotating wheel, showcasing effortless speed and precision.

- **Anomaly X24:** On April 24, 1953, faint flashes of light with oscillating movements were seen on the sea in the Gulf of Thailand. These flashes increased in strength and transformed into intensive rays of light rotating around three separate centers.

- **Anomaly X26:** On October 30, 1953, a display with curved spokes was observed in the Gulf of Siam, with a radius of 2 miles and spokes 100 yards apart one mile from the hub. The wheel was rotating at a speed of 20 rpm, engaged for a duration of 10 minutes.

- **Anomaly X28:** On November 12, 1953, in the China Sea, a close study of a wheel revealed that its rotation was due to a marquee effect. This peculiar observation suggested a unique interaction between light and the environment.

- **Anomaly X31:** On April 23, 1955, while approaching Jazirat Tunb Island in the Persian Gulf, the ship passed between two revolving phosphorescent wheels, one moving anticlockwise and the other clockwise. The seeing of concentric circles and lines resembling a wake further contributed to the surreal experience.

- **Anomaly X37:** On February 24, 1957, in the Gulf of Siam, an anticlockwise wheel was observed, followed by a second one three minutes later. After another three minutes, two oppositely rotating wheels appeared with bright flashes at their intersections.

- **Anomaly X56:** On November 6, 1978, in the South China Sea, bioluminescence appeared as rotating bands without a central hub. These luminous bands pulsated at a rate of two to three flashes every second, creating a mesmerizing light show.

- **Anomaly X64:** On November 14, 1949, in the Indian Ocean near Iran, the vessel entered a classic marine light wheel, characterized by pulsating light patterns. These lights revolved around the ship in a circular motion, resembling searchlight beams radiating outward from a central point.
# X65 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- The report by P. Newton on the m.v. Mahsuri describes pale green glow and blue-green light bands over the water in the Gulf of Oman. These lights appeared to be above the water surface and showed a pattern that raised doubts about bioluminescence as their origin.

- The wheels of light observed had diameters ranging from ten to over 600 feet, continuously flashing with successively diminishing intervals between flashes. It was noted that these wheels seemed to follow the ship, suggesting a potential influence of the vessel on the luminous display.

# X66 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- On April 30, 1981, a report from the North Atlantic Ocean detailed a white sea-smoke type mist combined with a large area of bioluminescence formed into defined spirals. The spinning bands of light were of great dimension, marking a rare occurrence of geometrically organized displays in the Atlantic.

- Despite the commonality of bioluminescence in the region, the observed geometrically organized displays were highly unusual. Once again, the luminous features appeared to be above the water, adding complexity to existing theories.

# X67 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- The encounter on April 29, 1982, aboard the m.v. Siam included perplexing displays of marine phosphorescence that featured multiple rotating wheels with their hubs positioned away from the ship. The luminous bands began rushing toward the ship and transformed into three counterclockwise wheels.

- After a gap in the display, additional parallel bands arrived, eventually resulting in more rotating wheels appearing. The phenomena associated with the ship did not seem to be influenced by radar or visible light, further complicating the understanding of the display's source.

# X68 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- On May 6, 1991, the m.v. Zidona observed a complex blue-white pattern of fast-moving light around the ship. This display featured pulsing rings and spiraling points of light, creating a mesmerizing mass of illumination that surrounded the vessel.

- Distinctive centralized points of spiraling patterns and rapidly increasing rings added to the chaotic visual experience. The phenomenon concluded after a series of changes, culminating in a gradual reduction of observed light.

# X69 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- On October 7, 1991, the m.v. Wiltshire reported a glow of bioluminescence illuminating the hull above the waterline. This included observations of large wheels passing the vessel, with both large and small wheels reported to be present.

- Despite various attempts to elicit a response from the bioluminescence, including shining an Aldis lamp, there was no significant change noted. The absence of typical radial spokes and the slower speed of the wheels remained notable features of this occurrence.

# X70 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- The April 30, 1994 report from the m.v. BP Argosy described an incredible mix of luminous phenomena observed in the Indian Ocean. As the vessel approached, the patterns rapidly transitioned from random movements to spinning spirals and concentric circles moving outwards.

- The entire area around the ship became encompassed by overlapping luminous displays. The complexity of the overlapping patterns further challenged existing theories concerning the source of the observed lights.

# X71 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- During an encounter on April 17, 1995, the m.v. British Reliance reported a phenomenon of multiple rotating wheels in blue phosphorescence. This included a variety of wheels spinning in various directions, creating a captivating visual spectacle surrounding the vessel.

- The interplay of randomness in the wheels' movement and the related atmospheric phenomena has spurred intrigue among observers and scientists alike, especially considering the unprecedented confluence of multiple features reported. 

# X72 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- On November 3, 1997, N. Fraser reported synchronous lighting patches around an oil-rig supply ship in the Andaman Sea. The brightly glowing patches seemed to pulse like reflections of disco lights, fascinating the observers and leaving them puzzled about their origin.

- Intriguingly, a concurrent comparison to strobe lights led to the interpretation of these pulsating patterns as spokes of a giant wheel. The brief duration of the display heightened the curiosity regarding the phenomenon's cause.

# X73 [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- Scientist P.J. Herring noted that many marine light wheel observations stem from steel-hulled vessels, but earlier reports from wooden ships indicate that engine vibrations are not the sole contributor to the phenomenon. An ironic contrast exists in the early occurrence reports from regions where light wheels are less common, suggesting prominence in the Indo-Pacific region.

- Herring's review indicated a geographical restriction where marine light wheels are primarily observed between certain latitudes, sparking inquiry into why significant portions of the ocean remain unreported. The bioluminescent origin of the lights is widely accepted, yet the patterns they form continue to be a mystery in marine sciences.
# Anomalies in Marine Light Displays [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Bioluminescent Organisms Emitting Light**
  - The disturbance caused by seismic waves is challenging to comprehend, particularly when considering the complex patterns observed, such as spoked wheels. An interesting observation is that in seismically quiet areas, such as the Gulf of Thailand, marine light wheels are still relatively common.

- **Interference Patterns from Vessel Bow Waves**
  - Surface waves can create interference patterns when they interact with a vessel's bow waves, potentially leading to unexpected visual phenomena. However, the existence of rotating, spoked wheels makes it difficult to fully explain these occurrences using surface wave interactions alone.

- **Illusions of Perspective**
  - Some theorists suggest that marine light wheels may only appear to certain observers due to perspective, leading them to see incomplete "half-wheels." Contrary to this theory, the consistent observation of complete wheels indicates that perspective may not be the sole explanation.

- **Influence of Vessel Engine Vibrations**
  - It has been proposed that the vibrations of a ship's engines might influence the bioluminescent patterns observed in nearby waters. Although the frequency of marine engines aligns with the flashing frequency of bioluminescent displays, the precise impact of engine noise on these patterns remains untested.

- **Role of Electromagnetic Waves**
  - There is evidence suggesting that electromagnetic waves are not required for the formation of marine light wheels, as numerous observations predate radar usage. However, it is known that radar can modify bioluminescent phenomena, leading to speculation about the separation between the causes of light emission and pattern formation.

- **Atmospheric Electromagnetic Phenomena**
  - Herring’s analysis of marine light wheel phenomena fails to consider the effects of atmospheric electromagnetic phenomena, such as plasma vortices or electric discharges. The mist-like appearance of bioluminescent displays and their elevation above the water's surface bear a resemblance to the characteristics of certain GLD phenomena.

- **Expanding Phosphorescent Rings**
  - This anomaly features rapidly expanding phosphorescent rings emanating from central points in the water, with multiple sources potentially contributing to the phenomenon. Observed rings often defy simple explanations, as they travel faster than water waves but slower than sound waves, suggesting unknown light sources and precise geometric patterns.

- **Complex Bioluminescent Displays**
  - Observations of bioluminescent displays have revealed instances of expanding concentric rings that exhibit unique patterns and colors, adding to the complexity of marine bioluminescence. The simultaneous appearance of varying displays raises questions about the mechanisms driving these phenomena.
# GLW6 Phosphorescent Patches Moving in Circles [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- Circular patches of light are observed moving in large circular patterns. Several displays can be active at the same time, suggesting a unique display of marine luminosity.

- The phenomenon may be related to the phosphorescent wheel but is more complex due to the difficulty in explaining how seismic waves could create these patterns of light. Observations show that this event has only been recorded once with a quality rating of 2.

# GLW7 Phosphorescent Spinning Crescents [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- Spinning crescents of light have been observed rotating around a ship, often reversing their direction of rotation while displaying bilateral symmetry. The origins of this phenomenon may be influenced by ship-created forces, like radar or engine noise, but the exact cause remains unclear.

- Many observations of this display do not show a typical ship-centered pattern, indicating a potential stimulation mechanism from the vessel itself. These patterns are variable and exhibit enough similarities to be classified under the same anomaly, earning a rating of 2.

# GLW8 Zigzag Phosphorescent Flashes [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- This phenomenon consists of zigzag flashes of phosphorescence moving across the sea's surface in a manner reminiscent of lightning. It has only been observed once, leading to a low rating of 3 due to the rarity of observations.

- The appearance of these well-defined zigzag patterns raises questions about their origin, as bioluminescent organisms typically produce simpler light patterns. The most plausible explanation is that this behavior results from cooperative bioluminescent activity or perhaps an unusual environmental stimulus.

# GLW9 Milky Seas [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)
- Milky seas manifest as extensive, brilliant phosphorescent displays that can cover vast ocean areas, resembling a field of snow at night. Sometimes lit from below or creating a luminous mist above the surface, milky seas can appear suddenly and last for hours.

- The phenomenon is assumed to arise from bioluminescent organisms, like bacteria, although no specific species has been identified. Observations show that despite extensive reporting of milky seas, water drawn from them frequently reveals no bioluminescence, indicating that much is still unknown regarding its causes and mechanisms.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **X35. September 16, 1977. Arabian Sea.**
  The phenomenon involved a reduction in bioluminescence intensity, disappearing entirely within half an hour. A sample of seawater taken was observed to be clear and normal, showing no response to fluorescent ultraviolet light.

- **X36. General observation. White water phenomenon.**
  This phenomenon resembles a luminous fog where the sea and sky seem to merge, causing a loss of distance perception. It creates an eerie and disorienting atmosphere for observers.

- **X37. General observation. Observations of white water.**
  Evidence suggests that sightings of the white water phenomenon are particularly concentrated in the Arabian Sea during August. This trend indicates a possible seasonal correlation to the phenomenon.

- **X39. Circa 1811. Indian Ocean.**
  An early record described the milky sea phenomenon as the ocean appearing like a thick solution of chalk, with no visible agitation. This phenomenon stood out for its lack of glowing or phosphorescent appearance, appearing as a calm, white sea.

- **X40. June 1854. Indian Ocean south of Java.**
  The ocean's surface was described as resembling snow, contrasting sharply with a black sky. This unsettling scene led the captain to express feelings of grandeur and impending doom.

- **X41. January 30, 1902. Indian Ocean.**
  The sea was described as resembling snow, with a black horizon creating a striking visual contrast. This observation adds to the tapestry of anomalous milky sea episodes.

- **X42. August 13, 1925. North Atlantic Ocean.**
  The Second Officer reported a white line approaching the ship at high speed, transforming the sea into a white spectacle punctuated by dark patches. The atmospheric conditions were noted to be unusual, with no sound or movement in the air or sea.

- **X43. October 1931. North Atlantic Ocean.**
  A milky sea phenomenon was reported during heavy swell in the Atlantic, where breaking waves left a white wake. This occurred in stark contrast to the dark sky, adding to the rarity of such occurrences outside the Indian Ocean.

- **X44. March 3, 1933. Kamaisi, Japan.**
  Reports indicated bright lights radiating from the sea following a significant earthquake, suggesting a connection between seismic activity and luminous phenomena. The exact relationship with milky seas remains unclear.

- **X45. No date given. Okhotsuk Sea, Japan.**
  A "mysterious light" was reported by the crew of a Japanese training ship, illuminating a significant area around the vessel. Water samples revealed bioluminescent organisms, indicating their potential involvement.

- **X46. November 14, 1961. North Pacific Ocean.**
  A report described a lengthy stretch of milky sea accompanied by unusual atmospheric conditions, including a ghostly haze. The brightness of celestial bodies appeared magnified, creating a surreal visual effect.

- **X47. July 31-August 1, 1981. Indian Ocean, Arabian Sea.**
  A sudden transformation of the sea to a milky-white state was documented, persisting through the night. This striking visual was consistent with observations of milky seas in the region.

- **X48. July 31, 1981. South Pacific Ocean.**
  The m.v. Oropesa passed through an area glowing with a pale, milky light, marking another instance of this phenomenon across different oceans. The event highlights the widespread nature of milky sea occurrences.

- **X49. August 1, 1981. Indian Ocean, Arabian Sea.**
  The m.v. Mena traversed an extensive area of milky sea for four hours, experiencing a similar phenomenon just a day later. The close timing but geographical separation of these milky sea events is intriguing yet warrants cautious interpretation.

- **X50. August 13, 1986. Indian Ocean, Arabian Sea.**
  The sea surface presented an intense white glow, creating a striking visual experience akin to viewing a photograph in negative. The report underscores the diverse manifestations of the milky sea phenomenon.

- **X51. September 3, 1989. Indian Ocean, Arabian Sea.**
  Observers described a horizon-to-horizon "snow scene," accentuated by a distinct line separating the milky sea from the surrounding water. This visual delineation adds to the uniqueness of milky sea experiences.

- **X52. August 18, 1990. Indian Ocean.**
  The sea was initially mistaken for low-lying fog until observations confirmed its white appearance. The gradual emergence and disappearance of the phenomenon further emphasize its captivating nature.

- **X53. January 25, 1995. Indian Ocean.**
  Observers described a whitish glow enveloping their ship, transforming the ocean into what resembled a field of snow. Despite examining the area, no visible effects on sea waves or visibility were noted.

- **X54. September 2, 1997. Equatorial Pacific Ocean, Molucca Sea.**
  Reports indicated a sea surface that looked like a giant expanse of milk, with a brightness that darkened the sky above. This observation adds to the instances of milky sea phenomena across diverse regions.

- **X55. July 16, 1996. Indian Ocean.**
  Observers noted an extraordinary brightness that made the ship's hull visible several meters beneath the surface. This depth of illumination contributes to the intriguing characteristics of milky sea phenomena.

- **X56. Overview. Milky sea phenomenon.**
  This phenomenon is relatively common, particularly in the northwest Indian Ocean. Despite numerous reports and observations, a clear explanation for its occurrence remains elusive, with most bioluminescent organisms being unable to produce the sustained glow characteristic of milky seas.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Radar and Phosphorescence Observations**
  Observations indicated a progressive form of phosphorescence around the ship, with radar in use at various times showing no visible effects on the radar screen. The strongest flashes of light were in patches approximately 50 feet long, but diminishing over time.

- **Phosphorescence and Radar Interference in the Arabian Sea**
  Upon switching on the radar, distinct patches of radar interference were noted alongside bioluminescence in the Indian Ocean. The radar problems persisted even when trying to adjust the ship’s heading, suggesting a possible electromagnetic disturbance.

- **Te Lapa (Underwater Lightning)**
  Te lapa is described as streaks of light appearing below the ocean surface, used by Polynesian navigators for guidance. It is noted that this phenomenon has a significant distance range, being best observed 80 to 100 nautical miles offshore.

- **V-Shaped Phosphorescent Displays**
  V-shaped patterns of phosphorescent light were observed moving across the ocean surface, possibly related to other phosphorescent phenomena. This anomaly may indicate complex bioluminescent behaviors linked to various environmental conditions.

- **Colored Rays Emanating from Ships**
  An extremely rare multicolored phosphorescent display with rays of various colors emanating from the observing ship was reported. Such a phenomenon is anomalous as typical phosphorescent displays are predominantly white or whitish-green.

- **Radar Detection of Phosphorescence**
  Radar echoes were observed that seemingly reflected from patches of marine phosphorescence, contrary to the general knowledge that such phenomena do not provide radar targets. The unusual echoes and the absence of other plausible explanations suggest a need for further understanding and investigation into this relationship. 

- **Deep-Sea-Vent Glows**
  Non-thermal radiation emitted from hydrothermal vents was documented by scientists using advanced instruments. The existence of light in a dark environment raises questions about its sources; chemiluminescence is considered the most likely explanation among several possibilities.
# Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Chemiluminescence**  
  Chemiluminescence, specifically sulfide oxidation, is considered a likely candidate for some underwater phenomena. However, the scientific community has not yet reached a consensus on this subject.

- **Unsolved Mysteries of the Deep**  
  There are numerous unsolved mysteries associated with the deep sea that continue to intrigue researchers. These mysteries highlight our limited understanding of the oceanic environment and its phenomena. 

- **Light at Deep-Sea Hydrothermal Vents**  
  The presence of light at deep-sea hydrothermal vents presents an anomaly that challenges traditional knowledge about light production in such extreme conditions. The exact mechanisms behind this light generation remain a topic of ongoing research and exploration. 

- **Light at the Bottom of the Ocean**  
  Anomalies regarding the light found at the bottom of the ocean continue to baffle scientists. This phenomenon raises questions about the types of biological processes and chemical reactions that might contribute to the generation of light in such depths.

# Catalog of Anomalies [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Cosmic rays**: Anomalies related to cosmic rays, specifically GLL16, suggest potential interactions with various atmospheric phenomena. These cosmic rays are fundamental to understanding high-energy particles and their sources in the universe.

- **Crown flash**: This anomaly, noted as GLL7, involves a sudden, brightening effect observed in the atmosphere. Crown flashes are often associated with specific meteorological conditions, leading to brief yet intense illumination.

- **Cyclic flashing**: Identified as GLL18, cyclic flashing pertains to periodic bursts of light. These anomalies can potentially indicate complex interactions within the atmosphere.

- **Dual discharges**: Referred to as GLL19, dual discharges involve simultaneous electrical events which can arise during thunderstorms. This type of discharge is important for understanding lightning behavior and related phenomena.

- **Elves**: Documented as GLA24-X3 and GLL1-X36, elves are an optical phenomenon associated with lightning strikes. They are characterized by a brief, luminous flash that occurs high in the atmosphere.

- **Figures**: GLL9 identifies anomalous figures that appear in different settings, suggesting various interpretations and explanations. Such figures often attract scientific curiosity due to their elusive nature.

- **Geographical preferences**: Listed as GLL13, this anomaly signifies patterns in how certain phenomena manifest in specific geographic areas. Understanding these preferences can lead to insights regarding environmental influences.

- **Horizontal phenomena**: GLL5 describes certain anomalies that exhibit horizontal characteristics, which may influence atmospheric dynamics. These horizontal designs can shape our perception of weather systems.

- **Meandering**: This phenomenon, classified under GLL1-X28 and GLL25, suggests irregular movement patterns in light or atmospheric phenomena. Meandering behaviors can impact observational studies in meteorology.

- **Neutron generation**: According to GLL30, neutrons can be produced through various atmospheric interactions. This anomaly is significant for understanding radiation effects in the environment.

- **Physiological effects**: Mentioned in GLL9 and GLL31, physiological effects arise from various natural phenomena and their impact on living organisms. Researching these effects is essential for health and safety in anomalous event areas.

- **Pinched phenomena**: GLL2 indicates specific anomalies where phenomena appear constricted or narrowed in nature. These pinched visuals can lead to unique atmospheric occurrences.

- **Pranks**: Listed as GLL11, pranks encompass light phenomena that may be misinterpreted or confused with intentional tricks. Studying these pranks can enhance the understanding of optical illusions in nature.

- **Residual wisps of flame**: GLL23 refers to lingering flames or light trails after atmospheric events. These wisps can enhance the study of combustion processes and atmospheric conditions following fires.

- **Ribbon phenomena**: GLL26 identifies anomalies that display elongated, ribbon-like characteristics. Understanding these ribbons can provide insight into fluid dynamics and movement in the atmosphere.

- **Rocket phenomena**: GLL1 indicates occurrences related to rocket launches that can produce various luminous effects. These phenomena can impact tracking and observational studies in aeronautics.

- **Shadowgraphs**: Recorded as GLL22, shadowgraphs represent visual anomalies created by contrasting light and shadow. This technique is useful for analyzing various atmospheric interactions and effects.

- **Slow phenomena**: As referenced in GLL15, slow-moving anomalies indicate gradual changes or movements in the atmospheric conditions. Understanding these slow phenomena is crucial for interpreting longer-term environmental changes.

- **Soundless events**: Anomalies noted in GLL4 and GLL6-X2 refer to sounds that occur without traditional effects like thunder. These soundless phenomena challenge typical expectations regarding sound propagation.

- **Sounds other than thunder**: GLL10 describes various anomalous sounds that are distinct from well-known thunder sounds. These can be pivotal for studies in acoustic phenomena and environmental conditions.

- **Spider effects**: GLL27 denotes anomalies that exhibit irregular, spider-like appearances or behaviors. This classification can lend insight into complex atmospheric mechanics.

- **Spoked effects**: Also recorded as GLL27, spoked anomalies suggest patterns resembling spokes that can occur during certain atmospheric conditions. Understanding these patterns aids in unraveling meteorological phenomena.

- **Sprites**: As noted in GLA24-X3 and GLL1-X30 to GLL1-X35, sprites are brief, luminous events occurring high above thunderstorms. These phenomena are significant for understanding electrical activity in the atmosphere.

- **Superbolts**: GLL17 references extremely powerful lightning strikes that are substantially brighter than typical bolts. Studying superbolts is crucial for understanding extreme electrical phenomena.

- **Tree preferences**: Documented as GLL8, anomalies associated with tree preferences explore how certain phenomena tend to cluster around specific types of trees. This information can provide insights into ecological interactions.

- **Tubular phenomena**: GLL24 identifies anomalies that exhibit tubular characteristics in their formation. Such shapes contribute to understanding atmospheric fluid dynamics.

- **Maracaibo lights**: Noted as GLN1-X1, these lights are a unique meteorological phenomenon observed in Venezuela. Their characteristics offer insight into local weather patterns and electrical activities.

- **Marfa lights**: Recognized as GLN1-X69, these mysterious lights in Texas have drawn attention for their elusive nature. They present an interesting subject for study on unexplained atmospheric phenomena.

- **Marine phosphorescent displays**: Classified under GLW, these displays refer to the bioluminescence observed in marine environments. They highlight the intersection of biology and physics in natural lighting phenomena.

- **Aerial phenomena**: Documented under GLW3 and several others, aerial phenomena encompass a range of light displays observed in the sky. Understanding these occurrences requires interdisciplinary study across meteorology, optics, and physics.

- **Circles**: Mentioned in GLW4-X65, circular formations in the sky or other contexts represent fascinating anomalies worth further analysis. Investigating these circles can yield insights into atmospheric dynamics.

- **Colored rays**: Identifying phenomena in GLW13, colored rays are typically associated with light refraction and atmospheric optics. Their study is essential for understanding visual atmospheric effects.

- **Effects of radar**: GLL7 and GLL10 note how radar can affect observational phenomena within the atmosphere. Understanding these effects aids in improving radar technology and its applications.

- **Expanding rings**: GLW5 describes anomalies that appear as expanding rings in physical events. Researching these rings can be pivotal for understanding wave phenomena.

- **Milky seas**: Referenced under GLW4-X73 and GLW9, milky seas occur in oceans due to bioluminescent bacteria and have unique glowing characteristics. These seas represent a merger of biological and physical phenomena.

- **Moving parallel bands**: Related to GLW2 and others, these bands suggest coordinated patterns in the atmosphere. Learning about their behavior contributes to broader atmospheric studies.

- **Patches moving in circles**: GLW6 identifies these moving patches, which indicate specific patterns in fluids or atmospheric occurrences. Exploring their dynamics offers critical insights into fluid motion.

- **Radar-detection anomalies**: GLL14 refers to unique instances when radar detects unexpected signals. These radar anomalies can inform studies about advanced detection methods and atmospheric activity.

- **Radar-induced effects**: GLW7 and GLL10 note phenomena influenced by radar technologies, enhancing the understanding of radar interactions with the environment. Such studies can improve forecasting and detection capabilities.

- **Spinning crescents**: Listed in GLL7 and GLL10-X3, spinning crescents refer to luminous anomalies exhibiting crescent shapes. These shapes can inform research into rotational atmospheric phenomena.

- **Stationary parallel bands**: GLL1 reveals phenomena that do not move and appear as parallel bands. Analyzing their stability can provide insight into atmospheric layering.

- **Te lapa**: GLL11 identifies this unique type of phenomenon associated with luminous displays. Understanding te lapa requires exploration into regional atmospheric conditions.

- **V-shaped displays**: Classified under GLW12, V-shaped light displays are notable atmospheric occurrences. These formations can reflect underlying meteorological processes.

- **Wheels**: GLW2-X40 illustrates luminous wheels that present unique atmospheric patterns. Researching these wheels could lead to advancements in the study of light phenomena in nature.

- **Meteors**: Referenced as auroral in GLA3, meteors associated with auroras pertain to specific celestial events tied to atmospherics. Their connection aids in understanding auroras more holistically.

- **Correlated with auroras**: Noted as GLA11, several phenomena correlate closely with auroral activity, enhancing interpretation of such interactions within the atmosphere. Understanding these correlations is crucial for atmospheric science.

- **Green meteors**: Identified under GLN2-X10, green meteors have unique characteristics and potential causes. Their study is vital for decoding color effects in meteoritic activity.

- **High-level meteors**: GLN2 indicates meteors that occur at high altitudes, affecting observational data. Tracking these can help refine meteor detection technology.

- **Low-level meteors**: Classed as GLM1, these events occur closer to the Earth's surface, significantly impacting local atmospheric studies. Understanding their effects is crucial for local environmental assessments.

- **Nebulous meteors**: GLA3-X11 refers to meteors displaying a nebulous quality, leading to various interpretations of their composition. Their study can expand insights into celestial phenomena.

- **Mineral lights**: Noted as GLD13 and GLN1-X54, these luminous phenomena are classified based on mineralogic compositions. Understanding mineral lights can reveal more about geophysical interactions and processes.

- **Min-Min Light**: GLL1-X70 highlights this intriguing light phenomenon, contributing to the variety of unexplained lights in atmospheric studies. Investigating its nature can expand knowledge in photometric effects.

- **Moon and auroras correlation**: GLA18 presents the notion that lunar phases can influence auroral activity. This correlation suggests deeper connections between celestial bodies and atmospheric behavior.

- **Mountain-top glows**: Identified under GLA6 and GLA8-X4, these glowing phenomena occur atop mountains, often associated with geological activity. Their study enhances understanding of how landscapes interact with environmental conditions.

- **Natural gas earthquake lights**: GLD8 refers to luminous events linked with seismic activity. Exploring these lights can yield valuable insights into earthquake phenomena.

- **Ignis fatuus**: Noted as GLN1, ignis fatuus represents an atmospheric phenomenon often confused with other lighting events. Studying these phenomena is crucial for distinguishing various types of lights.

- **Nekha Lights**: Listed under GLD7-X16 and GLN1-X73, these lights offer a local perspective on unexplained atmospheric phenomena. Their characteristics can have cultural significance as well as scientific interest.

- **Neutrons correlated with lightning**: GLL30 indicates a relationship between neutron production and lightning strikes, enhancing understanding of high-energy events. This correlation can offer new insights into storm dynamics.

- **New Madrid earthquake**: GLD8-X1 connects historical seismic events with atmospheric anomalies. Studying these events can provide context for understanding seismic activity and its effects.

- **Nocturnal lights**: Noted under GLN1, nocturnal lights encompass various phenomena observable at night, contributing to the ongoing exploration of unexplained nighttime occurrences. Their study provides insights into nocturnal natural events.

- **Nuclear-weapons tests phenomena**: GLL14 explores how nuclear tests can produce luminous effects observable in various forms. Understanding these phenomena can inform discussions about the impacts of nuclear experiments on the atmosphere.

- **Odor of the aurora**: GLA5 refers to the curious phenomenon where specific scents may be associated with auroral displays. Investigating these odors enhances the comprehension of human experiences related to natural phenomena.

- **Okanagan Arc**: Classified as GLA2-X44, this phenomenon presents a unique atmospheric display specific to the region. Understanding its origins can lead to insights regarding local climatic conditions.

- **Owls (luminous)**: GLN1-X89 identifies light phenomena associated with owls, which may influence local ecological perceptions. Studying these connections expands on the interplay of wildlife and atmospheric effects.

- **Palatine Light**: Noted under GLNi-X24, this light phenomenon presents unique observables that intrigue scientists and the public alike. Investigating Palatine Light can enhance our understanding of regional lighting anomalies.

- **Phantom ships**: As recorded in GLN1-X24 and GLN1-X42, these anomalies appear as ghostly vessels visible in specific conditions. Their study can provide insights into mirages and optical phenomena over water.

- **Phosphorescence**: Noted in various forms such as GLL13-X3, phosphorescence in different materials leads to various luminous effects observable in nature. Researching this phenomenon contributes to knowledge in photonics.

- **Phosphorescent seas**: GLW9 categorizes marine environments characterized by glowing waters due to bioluminescence. Understanding marine phosphorescence helps bridge biology and physical sciences.

- **Piezoelectric effect**: Listed as GLD8 and GLD13, this phenomenon encompasses the generation of electric charge from applied mechanical stress. Exploring piezoelectric effects enhances technology and material science understanding.

- **Plasmodes**: Identified in GLB15-X6, plasmodes represent simpler pathways for phenomena to manifest and structure. Studying their effects informs understanding of complex systems in nature.

- **Radar detection of marine phosphorescence**: GLW14 reveals how radar technologies can be used to observe luminous marine phenomena. This detection broadens applications for marine studies and environmental monitoring.

- **Radiation (cosmic)**: Correlated with thunderstorms as per GLA9, cosmic radiation interacts with atmospheric events in complex ways. Understanding these interactions is central to the study of both cosmic and earthly phenomena.

- **Radio propagation suppressed by low auroras**: GLA20 indicates how auroras may interfere with radio signals, affecting communication technologies. This suppression underscores the impact of natural phenomena on technology.

- **Rocket launches luminous phenomena**: GLA15-X19 to GLN2-X17 chronicles unique lighting events that accompany rocket launches. Investigating these phenomena assists in understanding the environmental impacts of space activities.

- **Rocks luminosity**: GLL13 classifies luminous behaviors exhibited by certain geological materials. Exploring these phenomena provides insights into the conditions that lead to luminescence.

- **San Francisco earthquake phenomena**: Documented under GLD8-X4, effects relating to this significant historical earthquake include various anomalous events. Their study contributes to the broader understanding of dramatic geological impacts.

- **Sartatoga Lights**: GLN1-X69 captures unique light phenomena associated with specific locations. Understanding Sartatoga Lights furthers the investigation into local mysterious phenomena.

- **Silver Cliff Lights**: Associated with GLN1-X66, these lights represent a phenomenon intriguing to both scientists and enthusiasts. Exploring these lights expands the scope of nocturnal atmospheric explorations.

- **Siberian meteor (Tunguska)**: This precursor phenomenon (GLA15-X2) has deep implications for understanding cosmic interactions. Investigating Tunguska can yield critical insights into the impacts of meteoric events on the Earth.

- **Sky brightenings**: GLA25 highlights phenomena that produce sudden illuminations in the sky reminiscent of dawn. These brightenings challenge conventional understandings of light in the atmosphere.

- **Sky flashes**: Noted in GLA14 and GLD12, sky flashes occur as rapid bursts of light in the atmosphere. Studying their behavior enhances knowledge about atmospheric ionization.

- **Sky glows**: Documented under GLA13 and GLD8-X1, these phenomena describe widespread luminous displays in the sky. Understanding sky glows aids in atmospheric optics research.

- **Solar activity linked to auroras**: GLA acknowledges the direct relationship between solar events and auroral displays. This correlation is crucial for understanding geomagnetic interactions.

- **Solar activity and thunderstorms**: GLA9 and GLL16 explore the connections between solar phenomena and electrical storm phenomena. This relationship is vital for advancing atmospheric electricity and storm dynamics.

- **Auroral sounds**: Noting unique sounds associated with auroras, deeper investigations can explain acoustic phenomena that accompany visual displays. This underscores the dual nature of auroras.

- **Spacecraft launch phenomena**: Examined within GLN2-X21, these phenomena encompass various visual effects during launch events. Understanding them can improve both observational and safety protocols.

- **Spooklights**: Classed as GLN1, spooklights refer to mysterious lights with unclear origins that often appear in geographical anomalies. Their study contributes significantly to the cataloging of unusual atmospheric events.

- **Stars twinkling during auroras**: This phenomenon, documented in GLA12, highlights the impact of auroras on the visibility and clarity of stars. Investigating this phenomenon adds to the wider understanding of light interaction in the atmosphere.

- **St. Elmo's Fire**: GLD6 notes this electrical phenomenon that can be observed in various conditions. St. Elmo's Fire is a fascinating example of atmospheric discharge occurring prominently during storms.

- **Storm lights**: GLD8 classifies lighting phenomena observed in storm conditions that accompany or follow significant weather events. This typology enhances the comprehension of storm behavior.

- **Sun pillars**: Noted under GLA1, sun pillars appear as vertical beams of light that can emerge from the sun. Their formation involves reflection and refraction of light, providing an interesting study of optics.

- **Superbolts**: GLD17 identifies the phenomenon of incredibly intense lightning strikes, significantly impacting electrical disturbances. Researching superbolts challenges traditional understandings of lightning behavior.

- **Te lapa**: Documented in GLW11, this phenomenon represents a collection of luminous displays tied to specific geographical phenomena. Its study relates closely to regional atmospheric influences.

- **Thunderstorms (bipolar nature)**: GLL28 notes the unique dual characteristics of thunderstorms, which can impact their behavior and formation. Exploring the bipolar nature enhances our understanding of storm dynamics.

- **Correlated thunderstorms with auroras**: GLA9 and GLL16 point to how electrical storms may relate to auroral activity. Studying these correlations enriches insights into atmospheric phenomena.

- **Gamma-ray flashes**: Noted in GLL29, these flashes may arise during electrical storms, presenting phenomena that challenge standard atmospheric models. Understanding these gamma-ray events is vital for high-energy physics.

- **High-velocity lights**: GLN2-X24 and GLN2-X25 document rapid light phenomena associated with severe weather. Their study is crucial for understanding the high-energy interactions in thunderstorms.

- **Neutron generation correlated with thunder**: GLL30 suggests a noteworthy relationship between neutron production during thunderstorms. This connection expands the understanding of atmospheric electric phenomena.

- **Tornado lights**: GLD10 and GLD11 categorize lights observed during tornado events, often indicating significant atmospheric disturbances. Exploring these lights offers insight into tornado dynamics.

- **Tornado pulse-generator**: Discussed in GLD10-X4, this phenomenon pertains to specific emissions connected to tornado activity. Studying these generators can enhance predictive modeling for tornado patterns.

- **Tri-State Spooklight**: GLN1-X63 references a localized light phenomenon known for its peculiar behaviors. The investigation of such spooklights reveals valuable patterns about the interactions between light and environment.

- **Tunguska Event**: The historical event correlated with glowing skies (GLA13-X29) prompts inquiry into the atmospheric impacts of extraterrestrial object impacts. Understanding this event broadens the scope of terrestrial and celestial interactions.

- **UFO phenomena (foo fighters)**: GLL14 characterizes mysterious lights often reported during wartime that remain unexplained. Investigating these phenomena drives interest in assessing aerial conditions and unexplained aerial phenomena.

- **Meteor-like objects**: GLM1 indicates various phenomena that mimic meteoric behaviors, prompting scrutiny in observational studies. This classification enhances understanding in both meteorology and physics.

- **Nocturnal lights (high-level)**: GLM2 mentions high-altitude lights appearing at night, contributing to the catalog of unexplained phenomena. Their study aids in improving night sky perceptions.

- **Waterfalls (luminous phenomena)**: GLD14 references unique optical spectacles produced near waterfalls due to various light interactions. Analyzing these effects can advance the study of hydrophysics and optics.

- **Waterspouts (luminous phenomena)**: GLD10 catalogues instances where waterspouts exhibit luminous properties, representing an intersection of meteorological phenomena and optics. Such studies can elevate understanding of storm dynamics.

- **Weather lights**: Documented under GLA15 and GLA16, this encompasses a variety of luminous anomalies linked to weather activity, raising questions about their origins. The exploration of weather lights expands the scope of atmospheric research.

- **Whirlwinds (explosive onset)**: GLD11 covers phenomena tied to whirlwind events that present surprising and rapid appearances. Understanding these explosive onsets can contribute to predicting whirlwind behavior.

- **White water**: Noted in GLW9, this phenomenon involves observation of luminous effects in turbulent water conditions. Understanding white water can yield insights into aquatic luminosity-related phenomena.

- **Will-o'-the-wisps**: GLN1 classifies these mysterious lights often reported in marshy areas as phenomena tied to environmental conditions. Studying these lights can help clarify misconceptions regarding their origins.

- **Wind and auroral streamers**: Recorded in GLA12-X17, this phenomenon details interactions between wind patterns and aurora displays. Understanding these interactions elucidates broader atmospheric processes.

- **Yakima Lights**: Noted in GLN1-X69 and GLN1-X71, these lights represent mysterious phenomena specific to the region, capturing interest for their unexplained nature. Investigating Yakima Lights contributes to the larger understanding of atmospheric lighting events.

- **Zeitoun Phenomenon**: GLD6-X17 denotes an unusual atmospheric light phenomenon in recent studies, prompting further exploration of its characteristics and origins. Understanding Zeitoun could enhance contextual studies of geographical light phenomena.

- **Zodiacal light**: GLA1-X3 and GLA15-X5 reference this astronomical phenomenon that involves a faint glow along the ecliptic plane, providing insights into solar system dynamics. Zodiacal light is essential for understanding dust and light interactions in space.

- **False dawn**: GLA25 highlights occurrences linked to atmospheric conditions creating pre-dawn light displays similar to dawn. This phenomenon challenges the understanding of the horizon and light dispersion.
# ANOMALIES IN GEOLOGY: PHYSICAL, CHEMICAL, BIOLOGICAL [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Biological-extinction events**  
  These events raise questions about the rate and causes of species disappearance. They challenge our understanding of biological resilience and the factors leading to mass extinctions.

- **Musical sands, ringing rocks**  
  This anomaly involves geological materials producing sound or music when disturbed. Such phenomena prompt inquiries into the physical properties of these materials.

- **Questions about oil's origin**  
  The origins of oil are still debated, with multiple theories suggesting biological or abiotic sources. Understanding this is crucial for energy sustainability and geological history.

- **Ice caves, frozen wells**  
  These formations offer insight into unique climatic conditions that can lead to their development. Their study can provide clues about past environmental changes.

- **Natural fission reactors**  
  Found in natural settings, these reactors showcase a unique occurrence of nuclear fission. Their presence in the geological record raises questions about natural nuclear processes.

- **Marine organisms and fossils found far inland**  
  This anomaly indicates the historical shifts in sea levels or land mass movements. It challenges current conceptual frameworks of geological timelines.

- **Siberia's frozen mammoths**  
  The discovery of these well-preserved specimens poses inquiries regarding their extinction and the climate of the era. Their analysis offers valuable data on ancient ecosystems.

- **Radiometric-dating problems**  
  Issues in accurately dating geological formations through radiometric techniques highlight the complexity of Earth's history. Such challenges can lead to misunderstandings about geological timelines.

- **Anchor ice, frazil ice**  
  These forms of ice present unique challenges to understanding glacial processes and aquatic ecosystems. Their formation informs scientists about temperature variations and hydrology.

- **Violent lake turnovers**  
  This phenomenon can lead to drastic environmental changes, affecting ecosystems in significant ways. Studying these turnovers helps in understanding climate interactions.

- **Flexible rocks**  
  The existence of rocks exhibiting plasticity under certain conditions has implications for our understanding of tectonic processes. This anomaly prompts investigation into the mechanical behavior of geological materials.

- **Origin of the oceans**  
  The origins of Earth's oceans are still not entirely understood, with theories ranging from volcanic emissions to ice comets. Each hypothesis has significant implications for the history of life on Earth.

- **Skipping in the fossil record**  
  Gaps in the fossil record pose challenges to reconstructing evolutionary history. Understanding these gaps raises important questions about the completeness of paleontological data.

- **Valleys of death**  
  Regions where animals are mysteriously found dead reveal complexities in environmental impacts, possibly caused by toxic conditions. Investigating these valleys helps to uncover unknown ecological risks.

- **Prismatic sandstone from Missouri**  
  The unique formations of prismatic sandstone capture interest due to their striking visual properties and origins. Studying them assists in unraveling the geological processes that shaped them.

# NEGLECTED GEOLOGICAL ANOMALIES [(top)](https://github.com/sovrynn/ecdo/blob/master/6-LITERATURE-MEDIA/corliss/)

- **Concretions and geodes**  
  The formation processes of these structures are not fully understood, posing questions about mineral deposition. Their diversity can provide insights into geological environments.

- **Tektites and microtektites**  
  These glassy fragments, formed from impact events, raise questions about their distribution and formation. They can serve as time markers for significant geological occurrences.

- **Erratic boulders and gravels**  
  The transport mechanisms of these materials challenge current models of glacial movement. They inform scientists about past climatic and geological conditions.

- **Polystrate fossils**  
  These fossils, which extend through multiple layers of rock, challenge conventional notions of sedimentation rates. Their study encourages re-evaluation of geological formations.

- **Bone caves and bone beds**  
  The concentration of fossil remains in specific locations prompts inquiries into past ecosystems and predator-prey dynamics. They reveal critical information about ancient biodiversity.

- **Giant basalt flows**  
  The origins and extent of these flows elucidate processes of volcanic activity and landscape evolution. They provide a link between climatic events and geological transformations.

- **Natural glasses**  
  The formation of natural glass offers insight into high-pressure environments and rapid cooling processes. Understanding their properties can lead to advancements in material science.

- **Surging glaciers**  
  Glacial movements can cause dramatic landscape changes, affecting local ecology. Their surging is influenced by climatic factors and provides insights into climate change dynamics.

- **Driftless regions**  
  These areas, largely untouched by glaciation, reveal the complexities of terrain evolution through non-glacial processes. They are key to understanding historic geographic formations.

- **Stretched pebbles**  
  The deformation of these stones raises questions about the forces acting upon them through time. Their study helps to understand past geological stresses and formations.

- **Inclusions in crystals**  
  The study of inclusions provides significant information regarding the conditions under which the crystals formed. This can reveal details about the geological environment at that time.

- **Rarity of fossil meteorites and tektites**  
  The limited occurrences of these objects challenge our understanding of the history of cosmic impacts on Earth. They play an essential role in identifying significant geological events.

- **Elevated erratics**  
  The presence of these boulders in unusual locations raises questions concerning prehistoric glacial movements. Their study contributes to the broader understanding of landscape evolution.

- **Stone rivers, rock glaciers**  
  These formations present a striking example of geological processes involving the movement and arrangement of large stones. They help to illuminate current understanding of erosion and deposition.
